Salah — Prayer
intermediate
8 min
The Sunnah Postures in Salah: Qawma, Jalsa, Iftirash, and Tawarruk
Describes sunnah postures with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah postures for sitting in salah, distinguish between the fard transition and sunnah completion in qawma, and explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the ruling of jalsa.

Core Idea
The sunnah postures enhance prayer's perfection; the jalsa is sunnah, not fard, per the relied-upon Hanafi view.

This passage explains the recommended actions (sunan) for rising from sajdah, sitting between the two prostrations, and the final sitting postures for men and women. It also highlights an important internal disagreement within the Hanafi school about whether the sitting between sajdahs is obligatory or recommended. By studying this, you will learn how to perfect your prayer according to the sunnah and understand the basis of the Hanafi position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four sunnah actions
List the sunnah actions mentioned: completing the qawma (rising to near standing), sitting between sajdahs (jalsa), the hand placement during jalsa, the foot posture for men (iftirash), and the posture for women (tawarruk).
These are all sunnah, not fard.
2
Distinguish fard transition from sunnah completion in qawma
The actual rising from sajdah is fard (transition), but pausing momentarily (the qawma) is sunnah. This distinction affects the ruling if omitted.
Abu Hanifa: transition is fard, continuation is sunnah.
3
Understand the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on jalsa
Abu Yusuf considered the sitting between sajdahs as fard, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani consider it sunnah. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) view is sunnah.
The difference arises from interpreting the evidence.
4
Note the postures for men and women
Men: iftirash (sit on left foot, right foot erect, toes toward qibla). Women: tawarruk (sit on left buttock, thighs stacked, right foot tucked under). These are based on prophetic practice and modesty considerations.
Women's posture is more concealing.
5
Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah)
If one omits the jalsa, the prayer is still valid according to the mufta bihi view (sunnah), but if one follows Abu Yusuf's view, the prayer would need to be repeated if omitted deliberately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و (تسن) (القومة) يعني إتمامها لأن الرفع من السجود فرض إلى قرب القعود فاتمامه سنة (و) تسن الجلسة بين السجدتين و يسن (وضع اليدين على الفخذين) حال الجلسة (فيما بين السجدتين) فيكون كحالة التشهد كما فعله النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم ولا يأخذ الركبة هو الأرض، بحيث تكون أطراف أصابعه على حرفي ركبتيه لا مباعدة عنهما كما في الفتح. وتضع الأصح (و) يسن (افتراش) الرجل رجله اليسرى ونصب (اليمنى وتوجيه أصابعها نحو القبلة كما ورد عن ابن عمر رضي الله تعالى عنهما (و) يسن (تورك المرأة) بأن تجلس على أليتها الفخذ على الفخذ وتخرج رجلها من تحت وركها اليمنى لأنه أستر لها قوله : (لأن الرفع) في مجمع الأنهر عن المطلب الصحيح من مذهب الإمام أن الإنتقال فرض، والرفع سنة . قوله : ( وتسن الجلسة بين السجدتين المراد بها الطمأنينة في القومة، وتفترض عند أبي يوسف، ومقدار الجلوس عندنا بين السجدتين مقدار تسبيحة، وليس فيه ذكر مسنون ا في السراج، وكذا ليس بعد الرفع من الركوع دعاء، وما ورد فيهما محمول على التهجد الأنهر قوله : (كما فعله النبي بحيث تكون أطراف أصابعه على حرفي كما في مجمع قوله : ( وتوجيه أصابعها) أي باطن أصابع رجله اليمني نحو القبلة بقدر الإستطاعة فإن توجيه الخنصر لا يخلو عن عسر قهستاني
And it is sunnah to complete the qawma (standing), because rising from sajdah is fard to near sitting, so completing it is sunnah. And it is sunnah to have the jalsa (sitting) between the two sajdahs, and it is sunnah to place the hands on the thighs during the sitting between the two sajdahs, so it is like the state of tashahhud, as the Prophet did, and he did not put his knee on the ground, such that the tips of his fingers are on the edges of his knees, not separated from them as in al-Fath. And it is sunnah for a man to spread his left foot and erect the right foot and direct its toes towards the qibla, as reported from Ibn Umar, may Allah be pleased with them both. And it is sunnah for a woman to sit on her left buttock, thigh on thigh, and put her right foot out from under her right hip, because it is more concealing for her. His statement: 'because rising' in Majma' al-Anhur from al-Matlab: the correct view of the Imam is that the transition is fard and the rising is sunnah. His statement: 'and it is sunnah the sitting between the two sajdahs' means tranquility in the qawma, and it is considered fard by Abu Yusuf, and the amount of sitting between the two sajdahs in our view is the time of one tasbihah, and there is no mention of a supererogatory dhikr in it in al-Siraj, and likewise there is no dua after rising from ruku, and what is narrated about them is interpreted as for tahajjud. His statement: 'as the Prophet did such that the tips of his fingers are on the edges of his knees' as in Majma'. His statement: 'and directing its toes' meaning the inner side of the toes of his right foot towards the qibla as much as possible, for directing the little toe is not free from difficulty as in al-Qahistani.
Source pages p. 268

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof covering several sunnan related to qawma (rising to standing after sajdah), jalsa (sitting between sajdahs), and the postures for men (iftirash) and women (tawarruk) during tashahhud. The text states that completing the qawma (i.e., sitting momentarily) is sunnah, although the actual rising from sajdah (the transition) is fard according to Abu Hanifa, while Abu Yusuf considers the sitting itself fard. The sitting between sajdahs includes placing the hands on the thighs with fingertips on the knees, imitating the Prophet. Men are to sit on the left foot, erect the right foot, and point the toes toward the qibla. Women sit on the left buttock, stacking thighs, and tucking the right foot under the hip for greater modesty. The commentary clarifies the differing views on the qawma and notes that no specific dhikr is prescribed in the jalsa, and post-ruku dua is for tahajjud, not obligatory prayer.

Key Terms
القومة (al-qawmah): The brief standing or sitting after rising from sajdah before prostrating again.
الافتراش (al-iftirāsh): Sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect.
التورك (al-tawarruk): Sitting on the left buttock with thighs stacked and right foot tucked under.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the ruling of the sitting (jalsa) between the two sajdahs?
A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
The sunnah postures in salah enhance perfection; the jalsa is sunnah per the relied-upon Hanafi view, and omitting it does not invalidate the prayer.

Sunnah of Pointing the Index Finger in Tashahhud
Critical refinement establishing the sunnah of ishara and refuting dissent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pointing the index finger in tashahhud, identify its evidentiary basis, and distinguish it from the opposing view.

Core Idea
Pointing the finger in tashahhud is sunnah; moving it is makruh.

The act of raising the right index finger during tashahhud is a well-established sunnah in the Hanafi school, supported by hadith and the practice of the Companions. This lesson examines the ruling, its proofs, and the school's response to those who reject it. Understanding this helps ensure one's prayer aligns with the sunnah without falling into disliked innovation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what act is being ruled upon (pointing the index finger) and what the hukm level is (sunnah muakkadah). Note the condition: it is done without moving the finger, as moving is makruh.
The text explicitly says 'tusannu al-isharah' (it is sunnah to point).
2
Locate the dalil (proof)
Find the primary evidence cited: the hadith 'One, one' from Abu Hurairah (the Prophet told a man to use only one finger) and the hadith that pointing is more severe for Satan than iron.
These are ahad hadith, but the Hanafi school considers them sufficient for establishing sunnah.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof structure
Understand how the proofs are linked: the hadith about 'One, one' indicates using one finger, and the comparison with iron shows its merit. The practice of the Prophet and Companions confirms it as sunnah.
The text also provides a rational argument: raising the finger symbolizes negation (la ilaha) and lowering it indicates affirmation (illa Allah).
4
Identify the opposition
Recognize that some scholars held that no pointing should be done at all, perhaps deeming it a bid'ah. The text states this view is 'khilaf al-riwayah wa al-dirayah' (contrary to narration and reason).
The Hanafi madhhab rejects this opinion due to abundant narrations.
5
Note the Hanafi response and practical outcome
The Hanafi response is that pointing is affirmed by multiple hadith and the agreement of the early scholars (Kufans, Medinans). The practical consequence is that Hanafis point with the right index finger without moving, while some others do not.
The mufta bihi position is that pointing is sunnah and moving is makruh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و (تسن الإشارة في الصحيح) لأنه رفع أصبعه السبابة، وقد أحناها شيئاً . ومن قال : أنه لا يشير أصلاً فهو خلاف الرواية والدراية وتكون بالمسبحة) أي السبابة من اليمنى فقط يشير بها (عند انتهائه إلى (الشهادة) في التشهد لقول أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه : إنّ رجلاً كان بدعو بإصبعيه فقال له النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم : أحد أحد أخرجه أبو داود والنسائي وغيرهما قوله: (وتسن الإشارة) أي من غير تحريك فإنه مكروه وعندنا كذا في شرح المشكاة للقاري، وتكون إشارته إلى جهة القبلة كما يؤخذ من كلامهم قوله : (فهو خلاف الرواية لأنه روي في عدة أخبار منها ما أخرجه ابن السكن في صحيحه عن ابن عمر قال : قال رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم: الإشارة بالإصبع أشد على الشيطان من الحديد والمذكور في كيفية الإشارة قول أصحابنا الثلاثة كما في الفتح، وغيره فلا جرم إن قال الزاهدي في المجتبى : لما اتفقت الروايات عن أصحابنا جميعاً في كونها سنة، وكذا عن الكوفيين والمدنيين وكثرة الأخبار والآثار كان العمل بها أولى كما في الحلبي، وابن أمير حاج. قوله : (والدارية) لأنّ الفعل يوافق القول فكما أن القول فيه التقي، والإثبات يكون الفعل كذلك فرفع الأصبع النفي، ووضعه الإثبات قوله: (وتكون بالمسبحة) بكسر الباء الموحدة سميت بذلك لأنه يشار بها في التوحيد، وهو تسبيح أي تنزيه عن الشركاء، وخصت بذلك لأن لها إتصالاً بنياط القلب فكأنها سبب لحضوره قوله : (أي السبابة) سميت بذلك لأنها يشار بها عند السب وقيل يكره تسميتها بذلك ورده ابن أمير حاج بأن تسميتها بذلك ثبتت عند مسلم وغيره من حديث ابن عمر حيث قال : وأشار بالسبابة قوله : (عند انتهائه إلى عندها لا عند الإنتهاء إليها فلو أبقى المصنف على حاله لكان أولى. قوله : ( لقول أبي هريرة دليل لقوله من اليمنى فقط. قوله : (يدعو بإصبعيه) أي بكلتا مسبحتيه من يديه . فرع لا يشير بغير المسبحة حتى لو كانت مقطوعة، أو عليلة لم يشر بغيرها من أصابع اليمنى، ولا اليسرى كما في النووي على مسلم قوله : (أحد أحد ) بتشديد الحاء المهملة المكسورة أي وحد أي أقم إصبعاً واحدة وهي اليمنى لأن التيامن بطلب فيما له شرف، وكان يحبه في شأنه كله وهذا الدليل لا ينتج المدعي لأنه في الدعاء في التشهد
And it is sunnah, in the correct view, to point, because he raised his index finger and slightly bent it. And whoever says that he does not point at all is contrary to narration and reason. And it is with the musabbihah (index finger), meaning the index finger of the right hand only, he points with it when reaching the shahadah in tashahhud, due to the statement of Abu Hurairah, may Allah be pleased with him, that a man was supplicating with two fingers, and the Prophet said to him: 'One, one.' His statement: 'and it is sunnah to point' means without moving it, for moving it is makruh, and this is our view as in Sharh al-Mishkat by al-Qari, and his pointing is towards the qibla as taken from their words. His statement: 'so that contradicts the narration' because it is narrated in several reports, including what Ibn al-Sakan narrated in his Sahih from Ibn Umar that the Messenger of Allah said: 'Pointing with the finger is more severe for Satan than iron.' And regarding the manner of pointing, the statement of our three imams is as in al-Fath and others. Hence al-Zahidi said in al-Mujtaba: since the narrations from all our companions agree that it is sunnah, and likewise from the Kufans and Medinans, and the abundance of reports and traditions, acting upon it is preferable, as in al-Halabi and Ibn Amir Hajj. His statement: 'and the rational' because the action agrees with the statement; just as the statement contains negation and affirmation, so the action does: raising the finger is negation, and placing it is affirmation. His statement: 'and it is with the musabbihah' (with kasra on the ba) named thus because one points with it in tawhid, which is tasbih, i.e., declaring freedom from partners, and it is singled out because it has connection to the core of the heart, as if it is a cause for its presence. His statement: 'meaning the sababah' named thus because one points with it in cursing; and it is said that naming it that is discouraged, but Ibn Amir Hajj refuted that because its naming is established in Muslim and others from the hadith of Ibn Umar where he said: 'and he pointed with the sababah.' His statement: 'when finishing to the shahadah' means when reaching it, not at the end of it; if the author had kept it as is, it would have been better. His statement: 'due to the statement of Abu Hurairah' is evidence for his statement that it is with the right only. His statement: 'he was supplicating with his two fingers' meaning with both of his musabbihahs of his hands. A branch: he does not point with other than the musabbihah, even if it is cut off or ailing, he does not point with other fingers of the right or left, as in al-Nawawi on Muslim. His statement: 'One, one' with tashdid on the ha, broken, meaning 'declare oneness,' i.e., raise one finger, which is the right, because preferring the right is required in matters of honor, and he loved it in all his affairs. But this evidence does not prove the claim because it is about supplication in tashahhud.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement regarding the sunnah of ishara (pointing the index finger) during tashahhud. The text establishes that pointing the right index finger without moving it is sunnah, and it refutes the opinion that no pointing is done at all. The evidence includes the hadith 'One, one' from Abu Hurairah and the hadith that pointing is more severe for Satan. The commentary clarifies that the finger should be raised when reaching the shahadah, not moving it, and that it should be pointed towards the qibla. There is discussion about the naming of the finger (musabbihah vs. sababah) and that only the right index finger is used, even if it is missing or injured. The passage also critiques a proof that was presented, noting that it does not actually support the claim.

Key Terms
الإشارة (al-ishārah): The act of raising the index finger during tashahhud as a gesture of tawhid.
المسبحة (al-musabbihāh): The index finger of the right hand, named because it is used to declare tawhid (tasbih).
السبابة (al-sabābah): The index finger, named because it is used for pointing (sabb) or cursing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi madhhab, what is the ruling regarding moving the finger during ishara in tashahhud?
A) Sunnah
B) Mubah
C) Makruh
D) Haram

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, pointing the right index finger without moving it in tashahhud is a confirmed sunnah; moving it is makruh.

Sunnah Sitting Postures for Men and Women in Prayer
Ruling on recommended sitting postures in salah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah sitting postures for men and women during tashahhud in Hanafi fiqh and identify the correct posture for each gender.

Core Idea
Iftirash for men, tawarruk for women: sunnah postures rooted in modesty and practice.

In the tashahhud of prayer, the way we sit is not arbitrary. The Hanafi school prescribes specific postures that are recommended (sunnah) for men and women. This lesson presents the two postures: iftirash for men and tawarruk for women, along with the reasoning behind the distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two postures
Locate the terms iftirash and tawarruk in the passage. Understand that iftirash involves sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect, while tawarruk involves sitting on the left buttock with legs tucked to the right.
The passage explicitly states 'افتراش' for men and 'تورك' for women.
2
Determine the gender assignment
Note that the ruling is gender-specific: men perform iftirash, women perform tawarruk. This is based on the principle of modesty (astar) for women.
No other gender is mentioned; the ruling applies to adult men and women.
3
Recognize the basis of the ruling
The text cites the narration from Ibn Umar (RA) for the man's posture and gives the reason of modesty (astar) for the woman's posture. Understand that these are sunnah acts, not obligatory.
The level is sunnah muakkadah (emphasized sunnah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويسن (افتراش) الرجل رجله اليسرى ونصب (اليمنى وتوجيه أصابعها نحو القبلة كما ورد عن ابن عمر رضي الله تعالى عنهما (و) يسن (تورك المرأة) بأن تجلس على أليتها الفخذ على الفخذ وتخرج رجلها من تحت وركها اليمنى لأنه أستر لها
And it is recommended (sunnah) for a man to spread his left foot and erect his right foot and direct its toes towards the qibla, as narrated from Ibn Umar (may Allah be pleased with them both). And it is recommended (sunnah) for a woman to perform tawarruk by sitting on her left buttock, placing thigh on thigh, and bringing out her foot from under her right hip because it is more concealing for her.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage states the recommended sitting postures during the tashahhud in prayer. For a man, the sunnah is iftirash: sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect and toes pointing toward the qibla. For a woman, the sunnah is tawarruk: sitting on the left buttock with feet tucked under the right side, which is more modest. These positions are based on the practice of Ibn Umar and the principle of modesty for women.

Key Terms
افتراش (iftirash): Sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect, toes pointed to qibla.
تورك (tawarruk): Sitting on the left buttock with legs tucked under the right side.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Iftirash (sitting on left foot with right foot erect)
C) Squatting on both feet
D) Sitting cross-legged

Key Takeaway
The sunnah sitting posture in tashahhud is gender-specific: iftirash for men, tawarruk for women, both being sunnah muakkadah.

Pointing the Index Finger During Tashahhud
Ruling and manner of pointing index finger in tashahhud, with proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on pointing the index finger during tashahhud, identify its proof, and distinguish it from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Pointing the index finger once at the shahada is sunnah mu'akkadah — one point, no motion.

This passage from Hashiyat al-Tahtawi explains the sunnah of pointing with the right index finger during tashahhud. It clarifies the correct practice based on the hadith 'One, one' and refutes contrary views. Understanding this ruling will help you perform salah correctly according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being discussed? Pointing the index finger during tashahhud. What is its ruling? According to the correct view, it is sunnah (recommended).
The text says 'tusann al-isharah fi al-sahih'.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof is cited? The hadith of Abu Hurairah that the Prophet (peace be upon him) told a man using two fingers: 'One, one' (ahad ahad).
The dalil is a hadith ahad, so its thubut is zanni.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How does this hadith prove the ruling? The instruction 'one, one' indicates that only one finger (the index) should be used, not two or more.
The word 'ahad' explicitly limits to one.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Do other schools disagree? Yes, the Shafi'i school holds that pointing is continuous throughout tashahhud, with movement. The Hanafi school restricts to a single point at the shahada.
The practical difference is in the number of points and movement.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
What is the practical consequence? In Hanafi prayer, the worshipper points once; in Shafi'i, the finger moves continuously.
This affects the performance, not validity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتسن الإشارة في الصحيح) لأنه رفع أصبعه السبابة، وقد أحناها شيئاً . ومن قال : أنه لا يشير أصلاً فهو خلاف الرواية والدراية وتكون بالمسبحة) أي السبابة من اليمنى فقط يشير بها (عند انتهائه إلى (الشهادة) في التشهد لقول أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه : إنّ رجلاً كان يدعو بإصبعيه، فقال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : أحد أحد يرفعها ) أي المسبحة (عند النفي) أي نفي الألوهية عما سوى الله تعالى بقوله لا إله ( ويضعها عند (الإثبات أي إثبات الألوهية لله وحده بقوله إلا الله ليكون الرفع إشارة إلى النفي والوضع إلى الإثبات ويسن الإسرار بقراءة التشهد وأشرنا إلى أنه لا يعقد شيئاً من أصابعه وقيل : الا عند الإشارة بالمسبحة فيما يروى عنهما
And pointing (isharah) is recommended (sunnah) according to the correct view, because he raised his index finger and bent it slightly. Whoever says that he does not point at all is contrary to narration and reason. And it is done with the musabbiha, i.e., the index finger of the right hand only, pointing with it when reaching the shahada in tashahhud, due to the saying of Abu Hurairah (may Allah be pleased with him) that a man was supplicating with his two fingers, so the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) said: 'One, one.' He raises it, i.e., the musabbiha, at the negation, i.e., negation of divinity from other than Allah by saying 'la ilaha,' and places it at the affirmation, i.e., affirmation of divinity for Allah alone by saying 'illa Allah,' so that raising indicates negation and placing indicates affirmation. And it is recommended to recite tashahhud quietly. And we have indicated that he does not contract any of his fingers; it is said: except when pointing with the musabbiha according to what is narrated from them.
Source pages p. 269

Commentary
This passage discusses the sunnah of pointing the index finger (musabbiha) during tashahhud. The Hanafi school holds that it is sunnah to point once at the shahada, not repeatedly. The pointing is done with the right index finger only, raised at 'la ilaha' and lowered at 'illa Allah', signifying negation and affirmation. The text cites the hadith of Abu Hurairah where the Prophet instructed a man to point with one finger (i.e., the index). It also clarifies that the fingers should not be clenched in a fist; the palm is open, and only the index finger is used. There is a minority view about contracting fingers, but the relied-upon position is openness.

Key Terms
المسبحة (al-musabbiha): The index finger, called this because it is used to point at tawhid (discharging partners from Allah).
الإشارة (al-isharah): Pointing with the index finger during tashahhud without moving it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) While reciting the entire tashahhud with continuous motion
B) Only at the shahada, raising at 'la ilaha' and lowering at 'illa Allah'
C) At the beginning of tashahhud only
D) With the left hand

Key Takeaway
Pointing the right index finger at the shahada in tashahhud is a confirmed sunnah, performed once with raising and lowering.

Making a Fist in Tashahhud: The Hanafi Internal Dispute
Explains the sunnah of raising the index finger and the intra-Hanafi dispute about making a fist.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on making a fist during tashahhud, explain the proofs of the Sahibayn, and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
The mufta bihi is to make a fist, but the earlier Hanafi position kept fingers open.

The tashahhud in salah includes specific hand gestures. While all Hanafis agree on raising the index finger at the negation and lowering it at the affirmation, a dispute arose about whether the other fingers should be clenched into a fist. This lesson unpacks that internal disagreement and clarifies the relied-upon practice.

Guided Path
1
State the base Hanafi position
Identify the ruling as stated in the base text (al-Quduri): the sunnah is to keep the fingers open, not to make a fist, except for the index finger raised for pointing.
This is from the matn that 'he does not make a fist of any of his fingers'.
2
Identify the opposing view (Sahibayn)
Note that Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, along with later scholars, held that making a fist is sunnah. Their evidence includes practical consideration and reported narrations.
This view is attributed to 'them' (huma) in the commentary.
3
Follow the Hanafi response and tarjih
The commentator (al-Tahtawi) notes that the fatwa is on the fist, and it is recommended by many mashayikh. The relied-upon position comes from al-Nahr and fatwa collections.
The tarjih favours the Sahibayn due to later scholarship.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: those who follow the base text keep the hand open; those who follow the mufta bihi clench the little finger, ring finger, and thumb into a fist while raising the index finger.
The middle finger remains relaxed in both views.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باصبعيه، فقال له رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم : أحد أحد يرفعها ) أي المسبحة (عند النفي) أي نفي الألوهية عما سوى الله تعالى بقوله لا إله ( ويضعها عند (الإثبات أي إثبات الألوهية لله وحده بقوله إلا الله ليكون الرفع إشارة إلى النفي والوضع إلى الإثبات ويسن الإسرار بقراءة التشهد وأشرنا إلى أنه لا يعقد شيئاً من أصابعه وقيل : الا عند الإشارة بالمسبحة فيما يروى عنهما (و) تسن (قراءة الفاتحة فيما بعد الأوليين) في الصحيح وروي عن الإمام وجوبها وروي عنه
With his two fingers, the Messenger of Allah ﷺ said to him: 'One, one,' and he raises it (i.e., the index finger) at the negation (i.e., negating divinity from all besides Allah by saying 'la ilaha') and puts it down at the affirmation (i.e., affirming divinity for Allah alone by saying 'illallah'), so that the raising indicates negation and the lowering indicates affirmation. And it is sunnah to recite the tashahhud silently. And we indicated that he does not make a fist of any of his fingers. And it is said: except at the time of pointing with the index finger according to what is narrated from them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And it is sunnah to recite al-Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs. According to the sound report, and it is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa that it is obligatory, and it is also narrated from him...
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling on making a fist (عقد) during tashahhud. The base text states that the sunnah is to raise the index finger at 'la ilaha' and lower it at 'illallah', and that one should not make a fist. However, the commentator notes a differing view: that one should make a fist except when pointing with the index finger. This view is attributed to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The preferred position (mufta bihi) is to make a fist, as stated in al-Nahr and many fatwa collections. The method is to clench the little finger, ring finger, and thumb, while pointing with the index finger and keeping the middle finger relaxed. The commentator clarifies that the fist is only during tashahhud, not before or after. The passage also transitions to the next ruling on recitation in the final rak'ahs.

Key Terms
المسبحة (al-musabbihah): The index finger, used for pointing during tashahhud.
العقد (al-'aqd): Making a fist by clenching the fingers, especially the little finger, ring finger, and thumb.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Keep all fingers open and relaxed
B) Make a fist with all fingers except the index finger raised
C) Point with the index finger and keep the other fingers loosely closed
D) Hold the hand as if in a fist, but without pointing

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on making a fist during tashahhud; the mufta bihi is to clench the hand, contrary to the base text's recommendation to keep fingers open.

Recitation in the Last Two Rak'ahs: Sunnah, Wajib, or Optional?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on recitation in the last two rak'ahs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on recitation in the last two rak'ahs and identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) ruling.

Core Idea
In the last two rak'ahs, reciting al-Fatiha is sunnah; omitting it for tasbih or silence is valid but less reward.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about recitation in the final two rak'ahs of prayer. While the sound report (zahir al-riwayah) holds it as sunnah, there are narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa suggesting it is obligatory or optional. Understanding the tarjih process helps you apply the correct ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its context
Recognize that the act is recitation (qira'ah) in the last two rak'ahs of a three- or four-rak'ah fard prayer. Determine what is being disputed: the hukm level of this recitation.
The first two rak'ahs have a different ruling (recitation is wajib).
2
List the three positions
From the text, identify: (1) sunnah (sound report), (2) wajib (narration from Abu Hanifa), (3) optional between Fatiha, tasbih, and silence (another narration).
All three are attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa within the Hanafi school.
3
Identify the evidence for each
The sound report (zahir al-riwayah) from Imam Muhammad supports sunnah. The wajib and optional positions come from other narrations (riwayat) attributed to Abu Hanifa.
Zahir al-riwayah carries more weight in the Hanafi school.
4
Apply tarjih (preference) and state mufta bihi
The later Hanafi masters (e.g., al-Halabi) preferred the sound report, making the mufta bihi that recitation is sunnah. The other narrations are considered weak or interpreted in light of the sound report.
Tarjih is based on reliability of transmission and consistency with principles.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
If one omits Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs and does tasbih or silence, the prayer is valid and no compensation is needed (since it's not wajib). However, it is better to recite.
If it were wajib, prayer would still be valid but require sajdah al-sahw.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الصحيح وروي عن الإمام وجوبها وروي عنه التخيير بين قراءة الفاتحة والتسبيح والسكوت (و) تسن (الصلاة) على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في الجلوس الأخير) فيقول مثل ما قال محمد رحمه الله تعالى لما سئل عن كيفيتها فقال يقول اللهم صل على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما صليت على إبراهيم وعلى آل إبراهيم وبارك على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما باركت على إبراهيم وعلى آل إبراهيم في العالمين إنك حميد مجيد، وزيادة في العالمين ثابتة في رواية مسلم وغيره فالمنع منها ضعيف والصلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم
According to the sound report, and it is narrated from Imam Abu Hanifa that it is obligatory, and it is also narrated from him that there is an option between reciting al-Fatiha, tasbih, and silence. And it is sunnah to send prayers (salawat) upon the Prophet ﷺ in the final sitting. One says as Muhammad (the narrator) said when asked about its manner: 'O Allah, send prayers upon Muhammad and the family of Muhammad as You sent prayers upon Ibrahim and the family of Ibrahim, and bless Muhammad and the family of Muhammad as You blessed Ibrahim and the family of Ibrahim, in the worlds, indeed You are Praiseworthy, Glorious.' The addition of 'in the worlds' is established in the narration of Muslim and others, so its prohibition is weak.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding recitation in the last two rak'ahs of a three- or four-rak'ah prayer. The sound report (zahir al-riwayah) is that recitation of al-Fatiha is sunnah. However, there is a narration from Imam Abu Hanifa that it is wajib (obligatory). Another narration from him gives a choice (takhayyur) between reciting al-Fatiha, tasbih (glorification), or remaining silent. The preferred view (mufta bihi) is that it is sunnah, as stated in the sound report. The tasbih should be equivalent to the length of al-Fatiha (about three tasbihat). Silence is permitted but discouraged. The passage then transitions to the ruling on sending salawat upon the Prophet in the final sitting, which is sunnah. The wording is given, including the addition of 'fi al-'alamin' which is established in Sahih Muslim.

Key Terms
التسبيح (al-tasbih): Saying 'Subhanallah' or similar glorifications, doing so a number of times equivalent to the length of al-Fatiha.
السكوت (al-sukut): Remaining silent for the duration of Fatiha (approx 3 tasbihat) instead of reciting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position, what is the ruling on reciting al-Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs?
A) It is wajib, and omitting it invalidates the prayer
B) It is sunnah, and omitting it is permissible with tasbih or silence
C) It is haram to recite any Quran in the last two rak'ahs
D) It is fard, and must be recited in every rak'ah

Key Takeaway
The sound report (zahir al-riwayah) establishes reciting al-Fatiha in the last two rak'ahs as sunnah; omitting it with tasbih or silence is permissible but less than the best.

The Sunnah of Sending Salawat in the Final Sitting
Ruling and wording of salawat in final sitting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on sending salawat upon the Prophet in the final sitting and recite the preferred wording.

Core Idea
Sending salawat upon the Prophet in the final sitting is a confirmed sunnah with a specific wording.

After discussing recitation, the text turns to another element of the final sitting: sending salawat upon the Prophet. This is a sunnah mu'akkadah according to the Hanafi school, and the preferred wording includes the phrase 'fi al-'alamin'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is sending salawat (durood) upon the Prophet in the final sitting (after tashahhud). The ruling is explicitly stated as sunnah.
The final sitting is after the last sajdah in prayer.
2
Learn the recommended wording
The passage provides the wording as taught by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani: 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad wa 'ala ali Muhammad kama sallaita 'ala Ibrahim...' including the phrase 'fi al-'alamin'.
This wording is well-known in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Authenticate the additional phrase
The text mentions that the addition 'fi al-'alamin' is established in Sahih Muslim and other sources, so objecting to it is weak.
Some earlier scholars objected to this phrase, but the strong evidence is for it.
4
Apply the ruling in practice
In your daily prayers, after the tashahhud (Attahiyyat), recite this salawat as part of the final sitting. It is a sunnah, not wajib, so if omitted, the prayer is still valid.
Some people mistakenly think it is wajib; it is not.

Sending Salawat in the Final Sitting and Its Six Categories
Explains sunnah of salawat and its six categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on salawat in the final sitting, classify its six categories, and identify the recommended position for a masbuq.

Core Idea
Salawat in final sitting is sunnah; its obligation varies by context from fard to haram.

Sending blessings upon the Prophet ﷺ is a revered act. In the Hanafi school, its ruling changes based on time and place. This lesson unpacks the sixfold classification, clarifies the sunnah in the final sitting, and addresses the issue of the latecomer (masbuq).

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling for salawat in final sitting
Find the explicit statement in the text: 'tusannu al-salat' (it is sunnah). This is the mufta bihi position.
Some earlier views considered it wajib, but the relied-upon position is sunnah muakkadah.
2
Understand the six categories
The text lists six categories: fard (once in a lifetime), wajib (whenever Prophet's name is mentioned), sunnah (final sitting), mustahabb (other times), makruh (in prayer except final sitting), haram (in prohibited contexts). Map each to its trigger.
The categories are a Hanafi classification not shared by all schools.
3
Apply the ruling for the masbuq
The latecomer should not recite salawat with the imam. The preferred view is to prolong the tashahhud (Qadikhan's view).
Other options: joining the imam, repeating shahada, or remaining silent are all considered weaker.
4
Note the disagreement on 'sayyidina'
Adding 'sayyidina' is recommended (al-Durr) but following transmitted reports without it is stronger (Ibn Hajar). The second view is preferred because the Companions did not use it.
This relates to the usul principle: adab vs ittiba'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتسن الصلاة على النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في الجلوس الأخير) فيقول مثل ما قال محمد رحمه الله تعالى لما سئل عن كيفيتها فقال يقول اللهم صل على محمد وعلى آل محمد كما صليت على إبراهيم وعلى آل إبراهيم وبارك شرح الشفاء للشهاب عن الحافظ ابن حجر إتباع الآثار الواردة أرجح، ولم تنقل عن الصحابة والتابعين ولم تر وإلا في حديث ضعيف عن ابن مسعود ولو كان مندوباً لما خفي عليهم قال وهذا يقرب من مسئلة أصولية وهي أن الأدب أحسن أم الإتباع والإمتثال ورجح الثاني، بل قيل أنه الأدب .اهـ. قوله: (كما صليت على إبراهيم لا يقتضي أفضلية الخليل عن الحبيب عليهما الصلاة والسلام لأنه قاله قبل أن يبين الله تعالى له منزلته فلما بين أبقى الدعوة، أو تشبيه لأصل الصلاة بأصل الصلاة لا للقدر، أو التشبيه وقع في الصلاة على الآل لا عليه فكان قوله : اللهم صلى على محمد منقطع عن التشبيه، أو المشبه الصلاة على محمد وآله بالصلاة على إبراهيم وآله ومعظم الأنبياء آل إبراهيم، فإذا تقابلت الجملة بالجملة بقدر أن يكون آل الرسول كآل إبراهيم كذا في الشرح، وفي هذا الأخير نظر، أو المشبه به قد يكون أدنى كقوله تعالى : مثل نوره كمشكاة [النور/ ٣٤] اهـ در والحميد المحمود فإنه المحمود بأنواع المحامد والمجيد بمعنى الماجد، وهو من كمل في المجد، والشرف، وتمامه في الشرح، أو الحميد بمعنى فاعل أي أنت فاعل الحمد أو واهبه كما أن مجيداً يحتمل أن يكون بمعنى المجد وقوله في العالمين أي معهم فهو دعاء لهم معهما، ومع داخلة هنا على التابع. قوله : (فرض) في العمر مرة إبتداء) أي من غير تقدم ذكر، ولو بلغ في الصلاة وصلى فيها بعده ثابت عن الفرض قوله : ( وتفترض كلما ذكر اسمه)
And it is sunnah to send prayers upon the Prophet ﷺ in the final sitting. He says as Muhammad (the narrator) said when asked about its manner: 'O Allah, send prayers upon Muhammad and the family of Muhammad as You sent prayers upon Ibrahim and the family of Ibrahim, and bless Muhammad and the family of Muhammad as You blessed Ibrahim and the family of Ibrahim, in the worlds, indeed You are Praiseworthy, Glorious.' The addition of 'in the worlds' is established in the narration of Muslim and others, so its prohibition is weak. And the prayer upon the Prophet ﷺ (know that it is of six categories: fard, wajib, sunnah, mustahabb, makruh, and haram. The first (fard) is once in a lifetime based on the verse. The second (wajib) is whenever his name is mentioned according to al-Tahawi, and it appears to be on the level of kifayah (sufficiency). The third is sunnah in the final sitting. The fourth is mustahabb at all possible times. The fifth is makruh in prayer except the final sitting and qunut. The sixth is haram at the time of a prohibited act, or when a merchant opens his goods intending to advertise their quality. There is no particularity for prayer; rather all states of dhikr indicate using dhikr in its improper place. Our scholars have stated this. Should the latecomer (masbuq) say it with the imam? It is said yes, and with supplication, and it is corrected in al-Mabsut. And it is said he repeats the word of shahada, and Ibn Shuja' chose this. And it is said he remains silent, and Abu Bakr al-Razi chose this. And it is said he prolongs the tashahhud, and Qadikhan corrected this. Fatwa should be given with this as in al-Bahr, and it is the correct view. And in al-Durr, it is recommended to say 'sayyidina'. In Sharh al-Shifa' by al-Shihab, from al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar, following the transmitted reports is stronger, and it is not transmitted from the Companions and Successors and was not seen except in a weak hadith from Ibn Mas'ud. If it were recommended, it would not have been hidden from them. He said this approaches an usul issue: whether good manners (adab) or following and compliance is better, and the second is preferred. Rather it is said that it is good manners. As for his statement 'as You sent prayers upon Ibrahim', it does not imply the superiority of al-Khalil over al-Habib ﷺ, because he said it before Allah had revealed his station. When it was revealed, he retained the supplication, or it is a comparison of the origin of prayer to the origin of prayer, not of quantity, or the comparison is about the prayer upon the family, not upon him, so his statement 'O Allah, send prayers upon Muhammad' is separate from the comparison. Or the comparison is the prayer upon Muhammad and his family to the prayer upon Ibrahim and his family, and most of the prophets are from the family of Ibrahim, so when the sentences are compared, it is such that the family of the Messenger is like the family of Ibrahim. This is from the Sharh, and there is consideration in this last view. Or the object of comparison may be inferior, as in the verse: 'The parable of His light is as a niche' (al-Nur 35). Al-Hamid means the praised one, and al-Majid means the glorious. His statement 'in the worlds' means with them, so it is a supplication for them with them. 'Ma' is included here for the follower. His statement: (fard) once in a lifetime initially, i.e., without previous mention. Even if one reaches (balagha) in prayer and prays therein after, it is established as fard. And it becomes fard whenever his name is mentioned.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage covers the ruling of sending salawat upon the Prophet ﷺ in the final sitting of prayer, which is sunnah. It gives the recommended wording including the addition of 'fi al-'alamin'. The text then classifies salawat into six categories based on different contexts: (1) fard once in a lifetime (by consensus of Quran), (2) wajib whenever the Prophet's name is mentioned (according to al-Tahawi, and it is a communal obligation), (3) sunnah in the final sitting of prayer, (4) mustahabb at all other times, (5) makruh in prayer except in the final sitting and qunut (because it disturbs the prayer's prescribed order), and (6) haram when said in a prohibited context, such as a merchant praising his goods. There is a discussion about whether a latecomer (masbuq) should recite salawat with the imam. Several opinions are given: yes with supplication (corrected in al-Mabsut), repeating the shahada (Ibn Shuja'), remaining silent (Abu Bakr al-Razi), or prolonging the tashahhud (Qadikhan). The preferred view (fatwa) is to prolong the tashahhud. The text also discusses the addition of 'sayyidina' before Muhammad's name: following the transmitted reports without it is stronger, though 'sayyidina' is recommended by al-Durr. The commentary explains the phrase 'as You sent prayers upon Ibrahim' does not indicate superiority, as it was said before revelation, or it is a comparison of the form of prayer not degree. The Divine names 'al-Hamid' and 'al-Majid' are explained. Finally, the text clarifies that 'fard once in a lifetime' means that even if one sends salawat in prayer, it fulfills the fard, but the obligation is triggered primarily by the first mention.

Key Terms
المسبوق (al-masbuq): A latecomer who joins the prayer after the imam has already begun.
السيادة (al-siyadah): Adding the word 'sayyidina' ('our master') before the Prophet's name in salawat.
الصلاة على النبي (al-salat ala al-nabiy): The act of sending blessings and peace upon the Prophet Muhammad ﷺ.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi classification, sending salawat upon the Prophet in the final sitting of prayer falls under which category?
A) Fard
B) Wajib
C) Sunnah
D) Mustahabb

Key Takeaway
Salawat in the final sitting is sunnah; it has six categories from fard to haram; for the masbuq, the preferred view is to prolong tashahhud.

Salah — Prayer

Six Categories of Sending Blessings on the Prophet in Prayer
Explaining the ruling, wording, and categories of salat on the Prophet in prayer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the six legal categories of sending blessings on the Prophet and explain why the mufta bihi position is mustahab, not wajib.

Core Idea
Sending blessings on the Prophet in the last sitting is sunnah; the view that it is wajib every time his name is mentioned is a minority opinion within the Hanafi school.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi ala Maraqi al-Falah clarifies the ruling of sending blessings (salat) on the Prophet during prayer. It introduces a comprehensive six-category typology and addresses an important intra-Hanafi dispute about whether it becomes wajib every time the Prophet's name is mentioned. By the end, you will understand why the relied-upon position is that it is sunnah in the last sitting and mustahab otherwise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six categories and their definitions
The passage lists six categories: fard (once in a lifetime), wajib (al-Tahawi's view), sunnah (in the last sitting), mustahab (at all other times), makruh (in prayer except last sitting and qunut), and haram (when engaged in a forbidden act or for worldly advertising).
2
Locate the ruling for the last sitting
The matn explicitly states 'تسن الصلاة على النبي في الجلوس الأخير' — it is sunnah, not wajib or fard.
This is the ruling from the matn, which is the basis for the Hanafi position.
3
Examine the dispute about 'wajib every time the name is mentioned'
Imam al-Tahawi held that it is wajib to send blessings every time the Prophet's name is mentioned. The majority of Hanafis, including al-Sarakhsi, consider this view weak and hold that it is merely mustahab.
The dispute is within the Hanafi school; the majority view is mufta bihi.
4
Analyze the dalil for the majority view
The ahadith commanding blessings are ahad (single-transmitter), which in Hanafi usul indicate recommendation (istihbab) rather than obligation (wujub).
The passage also notes that if it were obligatory, the Companions would have practiced it consistently, which they did not.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah) for a masbuq and for the wording
For a masbuq (latecomer), the preferred view is that he continues his own tashahhud rather than following the imam in the blessing. The recommended wording includes 'fi al-alamain' (in the worlds), which is confirmed in authentic narrations.
The addition 'in the worlds' is supported by Muslim's hadith, so prohibiting it is weak.

The choice between reciting al-Fatihah, tasbih, and silence. (And) it is sunnah (to send blessings upon the Prophet ﷺ in the last sitting). He says like what Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said when asked about its manner: he said: 'O Allah, send blessings upon Muhammad and upon the family of Muhammad as You sent blessings upon Ibrahim and upon the family of Ibrahim, and bless Muhammad and the family of Muhammad as You blessed Ibrahim and the family of Ibrahim in the worlds. Indeed, You are Praiseworthy, Glorious.' The addition 'in the worlds' is confirmed in the narration of Muslim and others, so prohibiting it is weak. Sending blessings upon the Prophet ﷺ. His statement: 'It is sunnah to send blessings upon the Prophet' – know that it has six categories: obligatory (fard), necessary (wajib), sunnah, recommended (mustahabb), disliked (makruh), and forbidden (haram). The first (fard) is once in a lifetime based on the verse. The second (wajib) is every time his name is mentioned according to al-Tahawi, but the apparent view is that it is on the level of sufficiency (kifayah) to achieve the purpose, which is to glorify him, as mentioned by al-Qurmani. The third (sunnah) is in the last sitting. The fourth (mustahabb) is at all possible times. The fifth (makruh) is in prayer except the last sitting and qunut. The sixth (haram) is when doing a forbidden act, and when a merchant opens his goods intending to advertise their quality – there is no specificity to prayer, rather all remembrances in all [situations] indicate using remembrance in an inappropriate place; our scholars explicitly stated this. Should the person who is preceded (masbuq) do it with the imam? It is said: Yes, and with supplication, and it was deemed correct in al-Mabsut. It is said he repeats the word of testimony (shahada), chosen by Ibn Shuja'. It is said he remains silent, chosen by Abu Bakr al-Razi. It is said he continues in the tashahhud, deemed correct by Qadikhan. It is appropriate to give fatwa accordingly, as in al-Bahr, and it is the correct view. The summary of his statement: 'O Allah, send blessings upon Muhammad' – it is said in al-Durr that it is recommended to add 'sayyidina' (our master). In Sharh al-Shifa' by al-Shihab from al-Hafiz Ibn Hajar, following the transmitted traditions is more preponderant, and it is not transmitted from the Companions and Successors, nor was it seen except in a weak hadith from Ibn Mas'ud – if it had been recommended, it would not have been hidden from them. He said: This is close to an usul issue: is good manners (adab) better or following and compliance? He preponderated the second. Rather it is said that it is adab. End. His statement: 'as You sent blessings upon Ibrahim' does not imply the superiority of al-Khalil over al-Habib (peace be upon them), because he said this before Allah revealed to him his status. When it was revealed, the supplication remained. Or it is a comparison of the essence of blessings (salat) to the essence of blessings, not of the quantity. Or the comparison occurred for blessings upon the family, not upon him, so his statement 'O Allah, bless Muhammad' is independent of the comparison. Or the thing compared is the blessing upon Muhammad and his family to the blessing upon Ibrahim and his family, and most prophets are from the family of Ibrahim. So when the sentence is juxtaposed with the sentence, it necessitates that the family of the Messenger be like the family of Ibrahim. This is in al-Sharh, and there is consideration regarding this last one. Or the thing compared with may be lower, like His statement: 'The likeness of His light is as a niche' [an-Nur 24:35]. End from Durr. 'Al-Hamid' is the Praised, praised with all types of praise. 'Al-Majid' means al-Majid (the Glorious), the one who is perfect in glory and honor. Its completion is in al-Sharh. Or 'al-Hamid' is in the sense of the one who praises, i.e., You are the doer of praise or its bestower, just as 'Majid' may be interpreted as al-Majd. His statement 'in the worlds' means with them, so it is a supplication for them along with the two, and 'with' (ma'a) is inserted here for the successor. His statement: 'obligatory (fard) once in a lifetime initially' means without prior mention, even if he reaches [the age of] prayer and prays in it, after that it is established from the obligatory. His statement: 'and it becomes obligatory every time his name is mentioned' – after [the statement] 'obligatory once in a lifetime initially and becomes obligatory every time his name is mentioned because of the existence of its cause (his name being mentioned)'. (And) it is sunnah (to make supplication) after prayer upon the Prophet ﷺ because of his statement ﷺ: 'When any of you prays, let him begin with praising Allah, the Mighty and Majestic.' That is the saying of al-Tahawi. Some said: The obligation is nested if the gathering is one, and one prayer suffices, like the prostration of recitation – if it were obligatory every time, it would lead to hardship. Halabi and others. The apparent expression of his 'it becomes obligatory' implies it is practical obligation (fard 'amali), whereas in the speech of others, the intended meaning is the technical obligation (wajib). Indeed, the ahadith requiring it upon his mention are ahad hadith, and they only indicate obligation (wujub) according to what is stated in al-Bahr. Al-Sarakhsi said in Sharh al-Kafi: The saying of al-Tahawi is contrary to consensus, and the general body of scholars hold that it is merely recommended (mustahabb), as in Ghayat al-Bayan, and it is the chosen view for fatwa as in al-Nahr. And his apparent view: even if he hears it from multiple people because consideration is given to the gathering of the listener, like recitation – unlike praise when His name is mentioned such as 'Azza wa Jall' – then it is obligatory to give praise anew each time. And if he mentions it a thousand times in the gathering, if he leaves it, he does not make it up. In al-Binaya from al-Jami' al-Saghir: one praise suffices for each gathering; for two gatherings, it is obligatory for each gathering, and if he leaves it, it does not remain as a debt upon him. As for responding to a sneezer, indeed praising Allah is obligatory each time. In al-Ta'arif (or al-Tafariq): one does not respond to a sneezer more than three if he continues sneezing, and if he does not respond to him up to three, one response suffices. Himawi on al-Ashbah. But it is decisively stated in al-Fath following al-Kafi that one response suffices in a single gathering, and for more, it is recommended. End. It is not obligatory upon the Prophet to send blessings upon himself based on the fact that 'O you who believe' does not include the Messenger, unlike 'O people' or 'O My servants'. Nahr. And it is specified from the saying of al-Tahawi: the first tashahhud, and sending blessings within the prayer, so sending blessings is not obligatory due to committing a disliked act in the first, and due to infinite regress in the second. It can be said regarding the first: it is possible to do it by performing it after finishing the prayer. His statement: 'the existence of its cause' – it is the mentioning of his name.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute. It begins by stating that sending blessings upon the Prophet ﷺ in the last sitting of prayer is sunnah, not obligatory. The author then provides the recommended wording taught by the Prophet ﷺ, which includes the phrase 'in the worlds' (fi al-'alamin). The commentator defends this phrase as authentically reported and refutes those who prohibit it. He then explains that the act of sending blessings has six legal categories: fard (once in a lifetime based on Qur'anic command), wajib (every time the Prophet's name is mentioned—a view of Imam al-Tahawi, though the commentator notes the preferred view is that it is a collective obligation or sunnah), sunnah (in the last sitting of prayer), mustahab (at all possible times), makruh (in prayer except the last sitting and qunut), and haram (when engaged in a forbidden act or for worldly advertising). A significant intra-Hanafi dispute arises regarding the second category: al-Tahawi held it is wajib to send blessings every time the Prophet's name is mentioned, but the majority (including al-Sarakhsi) consider this only mustahab, because the ahadith commanding it are ahad (single-transmitter) and thus only indicate recommendation (istihbab) in the Hanafi usul framework. The commentator sides with the majority, noting that the mufta bihi position is that it is mustahab. He also discusses whether a person who joins prayer late (masbuq) should follow the imam in the salat or make his own, mentioning several opinions and preferring that he continues his own tashahhud. Finally, he addresses objections to the comparison with Ibrahim (as): it does not imply Ibrahim's superiority, as the comparison may be in essence or scope, not in degree.

Key Terms
الفرض (al-fard): In this passage, fard refers to the obligatory act once in a lifetime based on the Qur'anic command to send blessings.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The technical obligation (wajib) according to some, but the commentator clarifies it is not actually wajib in the technical sense.
المسبوق (al-masbuq): A person who joins the prayer late and misses some rak'ahs with the imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Fard

C) Sunnah
D) Mubah

Key Takeaway
Sending blessings on the Prophet in the last sitting is sunnah; the view that it is wajib every time his name is mentioned is a minority opinion within the Hanafi school.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate

Recommendation of Supplication After Prayer and Its Conditions
Ruling with proof on dua after prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the sunnah ruling of dua after prayer, identify the conditions for its validity (Arabic language, permissible content), and apply the prohibition against praying for ordinary impossibilities.

Core Idea
Supplication after prayer is sunnah, must be in Arabic, and must avoid forbidden requests.

This passage from Hāshiyat al-Ṭaḥṭāwī explains the recommendation of supplication after prescribed prayers, its method, and prohibitions. It clarifies the meaning of 'dubur al-ṣalāt', the requirement of Arabic, and limits on content, based on hadith and Hanafi principles. Understanding these rules ensures one's dua aligns with the Sunna.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its proof
The text states 'yusannu' (it is sunnah) to make dua after prayer. The proof is a hadith where the Prophet ﷺ said the most heard dua is in the last part of the night and after prescribed prayers. Recognise that sunnah is a recommended act, not obligatory.
The hadith is a single narration (zanni al-thubut) so it indicates desirability, not obligation.
2
Interpret the phrase 'dubur al-ṣalāt'
The term 'dubur' can mean either the last part of the prayer before finishing or the time after finishing. The commentator allows both meanings.
This flexibility allows dua both during the final sitting and after salām.
3
Note the conditions of language and content
The text requires dua to be in Arabic; other languages are forbidden. Also, dua must not be for ordinary impossibilities (e.g., a heavenly table) unless by a prophet or wali, and must not ask for forgiveness for polytheists.
The prohibition on non-Arabic dua is because it 'conflicts with the majesty of Allah' – showing the sacredness of Arabic in worship.
4
Recognise the disputed issues
The text mentions that al-Qarafi considered praying for forgiveness for polytheists as kufr, but the correct view is that it is prohibited but not kufr. Also, it is permissible to pray for all believers for all sins out of compassion.
This shows that even though rational possibility exists, the act is forbidden due to textual prohibition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (ويسن الدعاء لنفسه ولوالديه المؤمنين وللمؤمنين والمؤمنات لما روي عنه لما قيل له أي الدعاء أسمع ؟ قال : جوف الليل الأخير ودبر الصلوات المكتوبة، والدبر يطلق على ما قبل الفراغ منها أي الوقت الذي يليه وقت الخروج منها، وقد يراد به ما وراءه، وعقبه أي الوقت الذي يلي وقت الخروج ولا مانع من إرادة الوقتين ،بحر، ويدعو بالعربية ويحرم بغيرها لأنها تنافي جلال الله تعالى نهر، ولا يجوز الدعاء للمشركين بالمغفرة، وكفر به القرافي والحق خلافه لقول البعض بجواز مغفرة الكفر عقلاً، ويجوز الدعاء بالمغفرة لجميع المؤمنين جميع ذنوبهم لفرض الشفقة على إخوانه، وهو أمر جائز الوقوع وإن لم يكن واقعاً ومن المحرم أن يدعو بالمستحيلات العادية كنزول المائدة إلا أن يكون نبياً، أو ولياً قيل، وكذا الشرعية كما في الدر، وأن يسأل العافية مدى الدهر، أو خير الدارين، ودفع شرهما إلا أن يقصد به الخصوص إذ لا بد أن يدركه بعض الشر، ولو سكرات الموت. قوله : (القوله صلى الله عليه وسلم الخ) المتبادر منه أن (۱) قوله وفي التعاريف في نسخة وفي التفاريق اهـ.
His statement: 'And it is sunnah to make supplication for oneself, for one's believing parents, and for believing men and believing women, because of what is narrated from him: when it was said to him: Which supplication is most heard? He said: The depth of the last part of the night and after the prescribed prayers. 'Al-dubur' is applied to the time before finishing them, i.e., the time that precedes the time of exiting from them, and sometimes it refers to what is after them, i.e., the time that follows the time of exiting. There is no preventing intending both times. Bahr. And he supplicates in Arabic, and it is forbidden to supplicate in other than Arabic because it conflicts with the majesty of Allah. Nahr. And it is not permissible to supplicate for forgiveness for polytheists. Al-Qarafi declared it as disbelief, but the truth is otherwise due to the saying of some that forgiveness of disbelief is rationally possible. And it is permissible to supplicate for forgiveness for all believers for all their sins, based on the assumption of compassion for one's brothers, and this is a permissible occurrence even if it does not happen. And it is forbidden to supplicate for ordinary impossibilities, like the descent of a table [spread], unless it is a prophet or a wali, it is said. And likewise for legal impossibilities as in al-Durr, and to ask for well-being for the entire age, or the good of both worlds, and the repelling of their evil, unless he means it specifically, for some evil must befall him, even the agony of death. His statement: 'Apparently his statement ﷺ etc.)' indicates that... Footnote: 'al-ta'arif' in some copies is 'al-tafariq'.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning supplication after prayer. The author states that it is sunnah to make dua after the prescribed prayers, especially for oneself, believing parents, and all believers. The evidence is a hadith that the most accepted dua is in the last third of the night and after obligatory prayers. The phrase 'dubur al-salat' (after the prayer) is explained: it can mean before finishing (i.e., during the last part of prayer) or after completion. The commentator, following al-Bahr, allows both interpretations. The actual supplication must be in Arabic; supplicating in another language is considered prohibited because it contradicts the majesty of Allah—this is a Hanafi position emphasizing the sacredness of Arabic in worship. It is not permissible to pray for forgiveness for polytheists, and quoting al-Qarafi who deemed it kufr, but the commentator clarifies that the correct view is that it is not kufr because it is rationally possible for Allah to forgive all sins, including disbelief. However, the Qur'an forbids it, so it is not permissible to do. It is permissible to pray for forgiveness for all believers, even for all their sins, out of compassion, even if not actually realized. Forbidden supplications include asking for things that are ordinarily impossible (like the descent of a heavenly table) unless the supplicant is a prophet or wali; and asking for continuous well-being without any evil, since some evil (like death pangs) is inevitable. The passage also touches on whether one can ask for 'good in both worlds'—it is permissible if not meant absolutely, because some evil must occur. The final remark clarifies a textual variant in the word 'al-ta'arif'.

Key Terms
دبر الصلوات (dubur al-salawat): The time after the prescribed prayers or the last part of the prayer itself.
المستحيلات العادية (al-mustahilat al-'adiyyah): Things that are ordinarily impossible, like the descent of a table spread from heaven, which is not a miracle.
الدعاء بالعربية (al-du'a' bi al-'arabiyyah): Supplication in Arabic; it is required (wajib) for the validity? Actually it says 'yuhramu' (forbidden) to supplicate in other languages, indicating it is prohibited and thus invalidates the act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the ruling on supplicating in a language other than Arabic after prayer?
A) Permissible if one understands it
B) Recommended
C) Forbidden because it conflicts with the majesty of Allah
D) Makruh but valid

Key Takeaway
Dua after prayer is a sunnah act that requires Arabic and avoidance of forbidden requests, especially praying for forgiveness for polytheists or for ordinary impossibilities.

Sending Blessings Upon the Prophet: Obligation or Recommendation?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on obligation vs recommendation of salat 'alan Nabi

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on sending blessings upon the Prophet, state the majority position (mustahabb), and apply the rule that one salat suffices for multiple mentions in a single gathering.

Core Idea
Sending blessings is recommended, not obligatory – a single utterance suffices for multiple mentions in one gathering.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiyah on Maraqi al-Falah addresses a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute: when the Prophet's name is mentioned, is it obligatory (wajib) to send blessings each time, or is it merely recommended (mustahabb)? The base matn suggests obligation, but the commentator clarifies that the relied-upon view is recommendation, and that one salat covers multiple mentions in one session. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to distinguish between fard, wajib, and mustahabb, and to see how later Hanafi masters resolved internal differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Base Text's Claim
The matn states that sending blessings becomes obligatory (taftarid) each time the Prophet's name is mentioned, due to the cause (the mention) having occurred. Recognize that this is the literal wording of the matn.
The matn is from Maraqi al-Falah; the hashiah is clarifying and refining.
2
Locate the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
The commentator attributes the 'obligation each time' view to Imam al-Tahawi. Then note that the majority of Hanafi scholars, including al-Sarakhsi, reject this and hold it to be mustahabb. This is the central ikhtilaf within the school.
Al-Tahawi is a respected Hanafi scholar, but his view here is considered weak.
3
Analyze the Majority's Proof and Response
The majority argue that the hadith cited (a solitary hadith) only indicates recommendation, not obligation, in Hanafi usul. Furthermore, requiring it every time would cause hardship (haraj), so obligations merge in one gathering, analogous to sajdat al-tilawah.
The principle that solitary hadiths indicate wujub only if there is a qarina for it, but here the qarina is absent.
4
Understand the Exception: Tashmit al-'Atis
Contrast the rule for responding to a sneezer: if the sneezer praises Allah, the response is obligatory each time. This shows that the rule for sending blessings is not applied uniformly to all obligatory acts.
The text also mentions praising Allah (like 'Azza wa Jalla) which requires separate praise each time.
5
Conclude with the Mufta Bihi
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the majority view: sending blessings is mustahabb, and one salat suffices for multiple mentions in one gathering. The obligation is only per gathering, and if missed, no qada is required.
This is the ruling that a mufti would give today.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بعد فرض في العمر مرة ابتداء وتفترض كلما ذكر اسمه لوجود سببه (و) . يسن (الدعاء) الصلاة على النبي الله لقوله عليه السلام: إذا صلى أحدكم فليبدأ بتحميد الله عز وجل (1). هو قول الطحاوي، قال بعضهم : يتداخل الوجوب إذا اتحد المجلس، وتكفي صلاة واحدة كسجود التلاوة إذ لو وجبت كل مرة لأفضى إلى الحرج حلبي، وغيره، وظاهر تعبيره يفترض أنه فرض عملي، والذي في كلام غيره أن المراد الوجوب المصطلح عليه فإن الأحاديث الواردة بطلبها عند ذكره أحاديث آحاد وهي إنما تفيد الوجوب أفاده في البحر، قال السرخسي في شرح الكافي : وقول الطحاوي مخالف للإجماع وعامة العلماء على أنّ ذلك مستحب فقط كما في غاية البيان، وهو المختار للفتوى كما في النهر، وظاهره، ولو سمعه من متعدد لأن العبرة بمجلس السامع كالتلاوة بخلاف الثناء عند اسمه تعالى بنحو عز وجل فيجب لكل مرة ثناء على حدة، وأن ذكر في المجلس ألف مرة ولو تركه لا يقضي وفي البناية عن الجامع الصغير يكفيه لكل مجلس ثناء واحد وفي المجلسين يجب لكل مجلس ولو تركه لا يبقى ديناً عليه، وأما تشميت العاطس، فإن حمد يجب لكل مرة (١) ، وفي التعاريف لا يشمت العاطس أكثر من ثلاث إذا تابع، وإن لم يشمته إلى ثلاث كفته واحدة حموي على الإشباه لكن جزم في الفتح تبعاً للكافي بأنه يكفيه في المجلس الواحد تشميت واحد وفي الزائد ندب اهـ، ولا يجب على النبي أن يصلي على نفسه بناء على أنّ يا أيها الذين آمنوا لا يتناول الرسول بخلاف يا أيها الناس يا عبادي نهر وبخص من قول الطحاوي: التشهد الأول، والصلاة في ضمن صلاة، فلا تجب الصلاة، لإرتكاب المكروه في الأول ،وللتسلسل في الثاني، وفيه أن يقال في الأول يتأتى فعلها بالإتيان بها بعد الفراغ من الصلاة
After [the fact that] it is obligatory once in a lifetime initially, and it becomes obligatory every time his name is mentioned due to the existence of its cause. And it is recommended to send blessings and supplications upon the Prophet, due to his statement peace be upon him: 'When one of you prays, let him begin with praising Allah Almighty.' (1) This is the saying of al-Tahawi. Some said: The obligation merges if the gathering is one, and a single prayer is sufficient, like the prostration of recitation, for if it were obligatory every time, it would lead to hardship. This is from al-Halabi and others. The apparent meaning of his expression is that it is a practical obligation. What is in the speech of others is that what is meant is the technical obligation, for the hadiths that demand it upon his mention are solitary hadiths, and they only indicate obligation according to al-Bahr. al-Sarakhsi said in Sharh al-Kafi: The saying of al-Tahawi contradicts consensus, and the generality of scholars hold that it is only recommended, as in Ghayat al-Bayan, and it is the chosen position for fatwa as in al-Nahr. And apparently, even if one hears it from multiple people, because consideration is given to the gathering of the listener, like recitation, unlike praising at His name like 'Azza wa Jalla, for which a separate praise is obligatory each time, and if mentioned in a gathering a thousand times and left, it is not made up. In al-Binaya from al-Jami' al-Saghir, one praise suffices for each gathering, and for two gatherings, a praise is obligatory for each, and if left, it does not remain as a debt upon him. As for the sneezer's response, if he praises, it is obligatory each time (1), and in al-Ta'arif, the sneezer is not responded to more than three times if consecutive, and if he is not responded to up to three, one response suffices, from al-Hamawi on al-Ashbah, but it is determined in al-Fath following al-Kafi that one response suffices in one gathering and for additional it is recommended. And it is not obligatory upon the Prophet to send blessings upon himself, based on that 'O you who believe' does not include the Messenger, unlike 'O people' or 'O my servants', from al-Nahr. And an exception from the saying of al-Tahawi is the first tashahhud and the prayer within prayer, for the prayer is not obligatory due to the dislikedness in the first and the chain in the second. It may be said regarding the first, its performance is possible by doing it after completing the prayer.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a hashiah commentary from al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah, dealing with the ruling on sending blessings (salat) upon the Prophet when his name is mentioned. The base matn states that it is obligatory (farid) once in a lifetime and becomes obligatory every time his name is mentioned. The commentator begins by noting that al-Tahawi is reported to have said it is obligatory each time, but then clarifies that this is not the majority view. A detailed intra-Hanafi dispute ensues: al-Tahawi's position is that the obligation repeats, but some say the obligations merge in one gathering, and a single sending of blessings suffices, likening it to sajdat al-tilawah where one prostration is enough for multiple recitations in one sitting. The majority of Hanafi scholars, as stated by al-Sarakhsi, hold that it is only mustahabb (recommended) and that the solitary hadiths cited for obligation only indicate recommended status. The relied-upon position for fatwa is that it is mustahabb. The text further discusses that the obligation only applies per gathering; if one hears the Prophet's name multiple times in one gathering, one salat suffices. However, for praising Allah (like 'Azza wa Jalla), each mention requires a separate praise. For responding to a sneezer (tashmit al-'atis), if the sneezer praises, the response is obligatory each time, though the number of responses is limited. The text also clarifies that the Prophet is not required to send blessings upon himself because the address 'O you who believe' does not include him. Finally, it addresses an exception to al-Tahawi's rule: the first tashahhud (which is wajib but not fard) and sending blessings within prayer (which would lead to a chain if made obligatory) are not considered to be part of the obligation; the response explains that in the first case, the blessing can be done after the prayer, resolving the issue.

Key Terms
صلاة على النبي (salat 'ala al-nabi): The act of invoking Allah's blessings and peace upon the Prophet Muhammad, typically phrased as 'Allahumma salli 'ala Muhammad'.
الوجوب (al-wujub): The category of obligation in Hanafi fiqh, distinct from fard. Here it refers to whether sending blessings is obligatory or not.
تشمت العاطس (tashmit al-'atis): Responding to a sneezer by saying 'Yarhamukallah' when the sneezer praises Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Hanafi scholars, what is the ruling on sending blessings upon the Prophet when his name is mentioned?
A) It is fard (obligatory) each time.
B) It is wajib (obligatory) each time.
C) It is sunnah mu'akkadah (recommended) and one salat suffices for multiple mentions in one gathering.
D) It is makruh (disliked) to send blessings multiple times.

Key Takeaway
Sending blessings upon the Prophet when his name is mentioned is recommended (mustahabb), and a single salat suffices for all mentions in one gathering—this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

When Du'a in Prayer Invalidates: The Criterion of 'Speech of Men'
Hashiah refining the ruling on du'a in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion to determine whether a specific du'a in prayer invalidates the salah or forfeits the obligatory salam.

Core Idea
Du'a in prayer must avoid any phrase that could be spoken to a human; such speech invalidates or forfeits the obligatory salam.

This lesson refines the general recommendation of du'a in prayer. While du'a after tashahhud is sunnah, the hadith 'Our prayer is not fit for any speech of men' restricts the wording. The Hanafi school develops a precise criterion: if the du'a asks for something that is not impossible to obtain from a human being, it resembles 'speech of men' and triggers invalidation or forfeiture of the salam. We will trace this rule through the passage's examples and its resolution of intra-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hadith and its scope
Locate the key hadith 'Our prayer is not fit for any speech of men' cited in the passage. Determine what it prohibits and how the Hanafi school interprets 'kalam al-nas'.
The hadith is found in the hashiah at the point where the author limits the general permission of du'a.
2
Define 'kalam al-nas' in Hanafi usul
Extract the passage's definition: du'a that asks for something that is not impossible to obtain from creatures (ghayr mustahil min al-ibad). Contrast this with du'a for impossible things (like a prophet asking for a table spread) which is still allowed.
The passage explicitly says 'مثل قوله اللهم زوجني فلانة... لأنه لا يستحيل حصوله من العباد'.
3
Distinguish between ma'thur and non-ma'thur du'a
Note the nuance from al-Halabi: transmitted (ma'thur) du'as never invalidate, regardless of content. For non-ma'thur du'as, the criterion of impossibility applies. This is a critical refinement.
The source states: 'واختار الحلبي أنّ ما هو مأثور لا يفسد مطلقاً'.
4
Apply the temporal consequence
Learn the two outcomes: if the prohibited du'a occurs before sitting for tashahhud, the prayer is invalid (batil). If after tashahhud but before salam, the obligatory salam is forfeited (yufawwit al-wajib), meaning the prayer is valid but incomplete.
The passage says: 'لأنه يبطلها إن وجد قبل القعود وقدر التشهد ويفوت الواجب لوجوده بعده'.
5
Examine controversial examples: rizq and debt
Work through the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on phrases like 'Allahumma urzuqni fulanah' (give me so-and-so as provision). Some scholars consider rizq impossible from humans (since only Allah provides), others see it as possible. The preferred view distinguishes: if the object is a specific person, it invalidates; if it is an act like Hajj, it does not.
The passage cites al-Khulasa: 'لو قال: اللهم ارزقني فلانة الأصح الفساد ولو قال: اللهم ارزقني الحج الأصح عدمه'.

Source Text
