أو (سراً للقراءة) مقدماً عليها فيأتي به المسبوق في ابتداء ما يقضيه بعد الثناء فإنه يثني حال اقتدائه ولو في سكنات الإمام على ما قيل ولا يأتي به في الركوع، ويأتي فيه بتكبيرات العيدين لوجوبها (لا المقتدي) لأنه للقراءة ولا يقرأ المقتدي وقال أبو يوسف: هو تبع للثناء فيأتي به ويؤخر ) التعوذ (عن تكبيرات الزوائد في العيدين) لأنه للقراءة، وهي بعد التكبيرات في الركعة الأولى (ثم يسمي سراً) كما تقدم (ويسمي) كل من يقرأ في صلاته (في كل ركعة) سواء صلى فرضاً، أو نفلاً (قبل الفاتحة) بأن يقول : الله الرحمن الرحيم وأما في الوضوء والذبيحة فلا يتقيد بخصوص البسملة، بل كل ذكر له يكفي (فقط) فلا تسن التسمية بين الفاتحة، والسورة ولا كراهة فيها إن فعلها اتفاقاً للسورة
مع من حالات أن أتى به أدركه في شيء منه أتى به وإلا لأنهر قوله : (مقدماً عليها)، وقال بعض أصحاب الظواهر، والنخعي، وابن سيرين يأتي به بعد القراءة لأنه تعالى ذكره بحرف الفاء، وأنه للتعقيب، وهذا ليس بصحيح لأن الفاء للحال، وتمامه في الشرح. قوله: (فإنه يثني حال إقتدائه) لا وجه لهذا التعليل قال في الشرح : ويثني أيضاً حال إقتدائه وإن سبقه به إمامه ما لم يقرأ، وقيل يثني في سكتاته، وهو أولى مما هنا، وكلامه يقتضي أن المسبوق يثني مرتين، وهو خلاف المشهور :قوله ولا يأتي به في الركوع أي لا يأتي بالتعوذ في الركوع، قوله: (ويأتي فيه بتكبيرات العيدين أي يأتي بها المسبوق في الركوع . قوله : (الوجوبها) ظاهر التعليل يفيد أنه لا فرق بين الركعة الأولى والثانية قوله : (ذكر له يكفي أفراد الضمير بإعتبار المذكور والأفضل في الوضوء التسمية على الوجه المتقدم فيه وفي الذبيحة بإسم الله الله أكبر. قوله : (للسورة) تقييده بالسورة يفيد الكراهة إذا أتى بها للآيات
Or secretly for recitation, placed before it, so the latecomer brings it at the beginning of what he makes up after the praise, for he praises while following, even in the imam's pauses according to what was said, and he does not bring it in bowing. And he brings in it the takbirs of the two 'Eids due to their obligation (not the follower) because it is for recitation, and the follower does not recite. Abu Yusuf said: He is a follower of the praise, so he brings it and delays the seeking of refuge after the additional takbirs in the two 'Eids because it is for recitation, and they are after the takbirs in the first rak'ah. Then he says the Bismillah secretly as before, and every reciter in his prayer says it in every rak'ah, whether praying obligatory or supererogatory, before al-Fatihah, saying 'Allah al-Rahman al-Rahim'. As for wudu and slaughtering, it is not restricted to the specific basmalah, rather any remembrance suffices for him. Only: the basmalah between al-Fatihah and the surah is not sunnah, nor is there dislike if he does it coinciding with the surah. With from cases that he brought it, he caught up with him in something of it, he brought it otherwise because [؟] his saying: (placed before it). And some of the outward scholars, al-Nakha'i, and Ibn Sirin said: He brings it after recitation because the Exalted mentioned it with the letter fa, and it is for succession. This is not correct because the fa is for the state. Its completion is in the commentary. His saying: (For he praises during his following) has no basis for this reasoning. He said in the commentary: And he also praises during his following even if his imam preceded him with it as long as he has not recited, and it was said he praises in his pauses, and that is better than what is here, and his statement implies that the latecomer praises twice, which contradicts the well-known. His saying: And he does not bring it in bowing means he does not bring the seeking of refuge in bowing. His saying: (And he brings in it the takbirs of the two 'Eids) means the latecomer brings them in bowing. His saying: (due to their obligation) the apparent reasoning indicates that there is no difference between the first and second rak'ah. His saying: (remembrance suffices for him) making the pronoun singular considering what is mentioned, and the best in wudu is the basmalah in the manner mentioned therein, and in slaughtering with 'Bism Allah Allahu Akbar'. His saying: (for the surah) its restriction to the surah indicates dislike if he brings it for the verses.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage is from the Hashiya of al-Tahtawi on Maraqi al-Falah, providing a critical refinement of the matn's rulings. The topic is the ta'awwudh (seeking refuge) and tasmiyah (saying Bismillah) in prayer, particularly for the masbuq (latecomer). The matn states that the masbuq recites ta'awwudh silently at the beginning of what he makes up, before the basmalah, and he does so while following the imam. He should not recite it in ruku', but he does recite the takbirs of the two 'Eids there because they are wajib. The muqtadi (initial follower) does not recite ta'awwudh because he does not recite Quran. Abu Yusuf differed, considering the muqtadi a follower of the praise. The ta'awwudh is delayed after the additional takbirs in 'Eid prayer. Then the matn discusses the basmalah: it is said silently before al-Fatihah in every rak'ah. In wudu and slaughtering, the specific basmalah is not required; any dhikr suffices. The basmalah between al-Fatihah and the surah is not sunnah, but if done intentionally for the surah, there is no dislike. The commentary refines these points, rejecting the view that ta'awwudh comes after recitation (based on the letter fa), and clarifying that the latecomer praises only once. The commentary also notes that the basmalah between Fatihah and surah is disliked if done for a different purpose.

Key Terms
التعوذ (al-ta'awwudh): Seeking refuge with Allah from Shaytan by saying 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytan al-rajim' before recitation.
التسمية (al-tasmiyah): Saying 'Bismillah al-Rahman al-Rahim' before al-Fatihah in prayer.
المسبوق (al-masbuq): One who joins the prayer late, having missed one or more rak'ahs, and must complete what he missed after the imam's salam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only in the second rak'ah

Key Takeaway
The masbuq recites ta'awwudh silently at the start of each missed rak'ah (after thana', before basmalah), and the muqtadi does not recite ta'awwudh at all; basmalah is sunnah before Fatihah in every rak'ah, but not recommended between Fatihah and surah.

Recitation After al-Fatihah and Ruku' Adab in Hanafi Prayer
Details the required recitation after al-Fatihah and the manner of ruku'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the wajib recitation after al-Fatihah, describe the sunnah actions in ruku', and apply the Hanafi ruling on combining tasmi' and tahmid for the imam.

Core Idea
Reciting a surah after al-Fatihah is wajib; tasbih in ruku' is sunnah; imam combines tasmi' and tahmid.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's Hashiya details the obligations and recommendations of recitation in prayer. It clarifies the minimum surah requirement after al-Fatihah, the proper manner of bowing, and the rulings on tasmi' and tahmid. Understanding these distinctions is essential for correct prayer performance according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mandatory recitation after al-Fatihah
Locate the ruling that after al-Fatihah, the worshipper must recite a surah from al-Mufassal or at least three short verses. Note that this is wajib, not fard.
Hanafis distinguish between fard (pillar) and wajib (obligatory but not pillar).
2
Determine the sunnah actions in ruku'
The passage describes the recommended manner of bowing, including takbir at the start, tranquility, and tasbih three times. Note that these are sunnah, not wajib.
Reciting Quran in ruku' is makruh.
3
Analyze the ruling on tasmi' and tahmid
The passage discusses whether the imam should say both tasmi' and tahmid or only one. Identify the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and note the mufta bihi.
Al-Tahtawi mentions that later scholars preferred combination.
4
Apply the follower's obligation
Based on the hadith, the follower only says tahmid when the imam says tasmi'. Understand the evidence.
This is agreed upon.
5
Evaluate the consequences of omission
Consider what happens if one omits the wajib surah or the sunnah tasbih. Refer to the Hanafi rules on sajdah sahw for an omitted wajib.
Omission of a sunnah does not require compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قرأ الفاتحة وأمن الإمام والمأموم سراً وحقيقته إسماع النفس كما تقدم ثم قرأ سورة من المفصل على ما تقدم (أو) قرأ ثلاث آيات قصاراً، أو آية طويلة وجوباً ثم كبر) كل مصل (راكعاً) فيبتدىء بالتكبير ابتداء لانحناء ويختمه بختمه ليشرع في التسبيح فلا تخلو حالة الصلاة عن ذكر مطمئناً مسوياً رأسه بعجزه آخذا ركبتيه بيديه ويكون الرجل مفرجاً أصابعه ناصياً ساقيه واحناؤهما شبه القوس مكروه والمرأة لا تفرج أصابعها (وسبح فيه) أي الركوع كل مصل فيقول سبحان ربي العظيم مرات (ثلاثاً) وذلك) العدد (أدناه) أي أدنى كمال الجمع المسنون ويكره قراءة القرآن في الركوع والسجود والتشهد بإجماع الأئمة لقوله نهيت أقرأ راكعاً وساجداً ثم رفع رأسه واطمأن قائماً (قائلاً سمع الله لمن حمده) أي قبل الله حمد من حمده لأن السماع يذكر ويراد به القبول مجازاً كما يقال سمع الأمير كلام فلان وفي الحديث أعوذ بك من دعاء لا يسمع أي لا يستجاب والهاء للسكتة، والاستراحة لا للكناية (ربنا لك الحمد) فيجمع بين التسميع والتحميد (لو) كان إماماً) هذا قولهما، وهو رواية عن الإمام اختارها في الحاوي القدسي، وكان الفضلي والطحاوي وجماعة من المتأخرين يميلون إلى الجمع وهو قول أهل المدينة وقوله (أو منفرداً) متفق عليه على الأصح عن الإمام موافقة لهما وعنه يكتفي بالتحميد، وعنه يكتفي بالتسميع (والمقتدي يكتفي بالتحميد) اتفاقاً للأمر به في الحديث إذا قال الإمام : سمع الله لمن حمده، فقولوا ربنا لك الحمد رواه الشيخان والأفضل اللهم ربنا ولك الحمد ويليه اللهم ربنا لك الحمد ويليه ربنا لك الحمد
Then he recited al-Fatihah, and the imam and follower said amen silently, its reality being making oneself hear as before. Then he recited a surah from al-Mufassal as before, or recited three short verses, or one long verse obligatorily. Then every prayer said takbir while bowing; he begins the takbir at the start of bowing and concludes it at its end to begin the tasbih. Thus no state of prayer is empty of remembrance. He is tranquil, levelling his head with his back, grasping his knees with his hands. The man spreads his fingers, stretches his legs, and bending them like a bow is disliked. The woman does not spread her fingers. And he glorifies in it, i.e., in the bowing, every prayer says: Glory be to my Lord, the Almighty, three times. That number is its minimum, i.e., the minimum of the complete sunnah. And it is disliked to recite Quran in bowing, prostration, and tashahhud by consensus of the imams, because of his saying: I was forbidden to recite Quran while bowing and prostrating. Then he raised his head and was tranquil standing, saying: Allah hears whoever praises Him, meaning Allah accepted the praise of whoever praised Him, because hearing is mentioned and acceptance is meant metaphorically. As it is said: The amir heard the speech of so-and-so. And in the hadith: I seek refuge in You from a supplication that is not heard, i.e., not answered. The ha is for pausing and rest, not for metaphor. Our Lord, to You is praise. So he combines the tasmi' and tahmid. If he is an imam, this is their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) saying, and it is a narration from the imam (Abu Hanifa) chosen in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. Al-Fadli, al-Tahawi, and a group of later scholars inclined to combination, and it is the saying of the people of Madinah. And his saying: or if he is alone is agreed upon as the most sound from the imam, agreeing with them, and from him he is satisfied with tahmid alone, and from him he is satisfied with tasmi' alone. And the follower is satisfied with tahmid alone by agreement, due to the command in the hadith: When the imam says 'Allah hears whoever praises Him', then say 'Our Lord, to You is praise'. Reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim. And the best is 'O Allah, our Lord, and to You is praise', then 'O Allah, our Lord, to You is praise', then 'Our Lord, to You is praise'.
Source pages p. 282

Commentary
This passage continues the rulings of prayer, specifically the recitation after al-Fatihah, the takbir for ruku', the tasbih in ruku', and the subsequent tasmi' and tahmid. The matn states that after al-Fatihah and amen, the worshipper recites a surah from al-Mufassal (the short surahs of the Quran), or at least three short verses or one long verse, which is wajib (obligatory) in every rak'ah for the imam and munfarid. Then he says takbir while beginning to bow, ensuring that the bowing is performed calmly with the back level, knees grasped, and for men, fingers spread; women do not spread fingers. The tasbih in ruku' is said at least three times. Reciting Quran in ruku' or sujud is makruh by consensus, based on the hadith 'I was forbidden to recite Quran while bowing or prostrating.' After raising from ruku', the imam says 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' (Allah hears whoever praises Him), and the follower and munfarid say 'Rabbana laka al-hamd' (Our Lord, to You is praise). The commentary discusses that the imam should combine both tasmi' and tahmid according to the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) and a narration from Abu Hanifa. The preferred phrasing for tahmid includes 'Allahumma Rabbana wa laka al-hamd' as the best form. The follower only says the tahmid, as per the hadith in Bukhari and Muslim.

Key Terms
المفصل (al-mufassal): The section of the Quran from Surah Qaf to the end, divided into long (tiwal), medium (awsat), and short (qisar) surahs.
التسبيح (al-tasbih): Glorifying Allah by saying 'Subhan Allah' or 'Subhana Rabbi al-'Azim'.
التسميع (al-tasmi'): Saying 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' (Allah hears whoever praises Him).
التحميد (al-tahmid): Saying 'Rabbana laka al-hamd' or 'Allahumma Rabbana wa laka al-hamd' (Our Lord, to You is praise).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the minimum recitation after al-Fatihah in a rak'ah according to the Hanafi school?
A) Three short verses or one long verse
B) Any verse from the Quran
C) Surah al-Ikhlas only
D) No recitation is required

Key Takeaway
Reciting a surah after al-Fatihah is wajib, and the imam should combine tasmi' and tahmid upon rising from ruku'.

Rulings of Sajdah: Takbir, Posture, and Tasbih
Explains the proper performance of sajdah, including takbir, posture, and tasbih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the proper performance of sajdah, including the sequence of movements, recommended positions for men and women, and the required tasbih.

Core Idea
Sajdah requires tranquility, proper limb placement, and at least one tasbih; women adopt a compact posture for modesty.

This passage from Maraqi al-Falah details the practical rulings of prostration in prayer. It covers the order of moving down, the position of hands and feet, the glorification, and the differences between male and female worshippers. Understanding these distinctions ensures valid and complete salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of actions
The passage outlines the order: takbir, placing knees, then hands, then face (forehead and nose). Note the completion of takbir when the forehead touches the ground.
This sequence is sunnah; rushing may invalidate tranquility.
2
Understand the requirement of tranquility and tasbih
The worshipper must be tranquil and say 'Subhana Rabbi al-A'la' at least three times, with the minimum being once.
Saying it three times is the recommended minimum.
3
Distinguish sunan for men vs women
Men separate their stomach from thighs and arms from armpits; women close their arms to their sides and stick their stomach to their thighs for modesty.
In crowds, men also close limbs to avoid harming others.
4
Note the sitting between sajdahs
After the first sajdah, sit with hands on thighs; no specific supplication is sunnah for this sitting (except in tahajjud).
The sitting is a rukn of the prayer.
5
Repeat for second sajdah and rise
Perform the second sajdah similarly, then sit briefly and stand for the next rak'ah.
The entire sequence is repeated for each rak'ah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(ثم كبر ) كل مصل (خاراً للسجود) ويختمه عند وضع جبهته للسجود ثم وضع ركبتيه ثم يديه إن لم يكن به عذر يمنعه من هذه الصفة (ثم) وضع (وجهه بين كفيه لما روينا وسجد) بأنفه وجبهته) وتقدم الحكم (مطمئناً مسبحاً) بأن يقول: سبحان ربي الأعلى مرات (ثلاثاً) وذلك أدناه لما تقدم (وجافي) أي باعد الرجل (بطنه عن فخذيه وعضديه عن إبطيه لأنه أبلغ في السجود بالأعضاء في غير زحمة وينضم فيها حذراً عن إضرار الجار (موجهاً أصابع يديه) ويضمها كل الضم لا يندب إلا هنا لأن الرحمة تنزل عليه في السجود وبالضم ينال الأكثر (و) يكون موجهاً أصابع رجليه نحو القبلة والمرأة تخفض) فتضم عضديها لجنبيها وتلزق بطنها بفخذيها لأنه أستر لها ، ثم رفع رأسه وجلس كل مصل بين السجدتين واضعاً يديه على فخذيه مطمئناً وليس فيه ذكر مسنون والوارد فيه محمول على التهجد (ثم كبر للسجود وسجد) بعده (مطمئناً وسبح فيه) أي السجود (ثلاثاً وجافي بطنه عن فخذيه وأبدى عضديه) وهما ضبعاء والضبع بسكون الباء لا غير العضد (ثم رفع رأسه من السجدة الثانية وجلس قليلاً ثم قام)
Then every prayer said takbir while going down for prostration, and concludes it at placing his forehead on the ground. Then he places his knees, then his hands, if there is no excuse preventing this manner. Then he placed his face between his hands, as we narrated, and prostrated with his nose and forehead. The ruling preceded. He is tranquil, glorifying, saying: Glory be to my Lord, the Most High, three times. That is its minimum, as before. And he distances, i.e., the man separates, his stomach from his thighs and his arms from his armpits, because it is more effective in prostration with the limbs, when not in crowding, and he brings them together [in crowds] fearing harm to the neighbor. He directs his fingers, and he closes them completely; it is not recommended except here because mercy descends upon him in prostration, and by closing he attains more. And he directs his toes towards the qiblah. And the woman lowers, so she closes her arms to her sides and sticks her stomach to her thighs, because it is more concealing for her. Then he raised his head and sat every prayer between the two prostrations, placing his hands on his thighs, tranquil. There is no recommended remembrance in it; what is reported is interpreted for night prayer. Then he said takbir for prostration and prostrated after it, tranquil, and glorified in it, i.e., prostration, three times, and separated his stomach from his thighs and bared his arms, which are the upper arms. Then he raised his head from the second prostration and sat a little, then stood.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage details the rulings of sajdah (prostration). The matn instructs: after ruku', every worshipper says takbir and goes down to sajdah, completing the takbir when the forehead touches the ground. The order is: knees, then hands, then face (forehead and nose) between the hands. In sajdah, one must be tranquil and say 'Subhana Rabbi al-A'la' at least three times. Men are to separate their stomach from thighs and arms from armpits, and keep fingers together (not spread). Toes should face the qiblah. Women are to bring limbs together for modesty. After the first sajdah, one sits between the two sajdahs with hands on thighs; no specific supplication is sunnah for this sitting (though some narrations exist for tahajjud). Then repeat takbir and second sajdah, with same provisions. Finally, raise head and stand for the next rak'ah. The hashiya adds that the woman's posture includes closing arms and sticking stomach to thighs for more concealment. The recommendation to close fingers is specific to sajdah because mercy descends there.

Key Terms
السجود (al-sujud): Prostration, placing forehead and nose on the ground in a prescribed manner during prayer.
الضم (al-damm): Closing the fingers together in prostration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Arms separated from sides
B) Arms closed to sides
C) Arms lifted above head
D) Arms spread on the ground

Key Takeaway
The sajdah must be performed with tranquility, proper limb placement, and glorification; men and women have distinct recommended postures.

Refinements on the Wording of Tahmid in Prayer
Critical refinements on tasmi', tahmid, and recitative errors.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the correct wording for tahmid after ruku' and explain the ruling on altering the recitation in prayer.

Core Idea
Changing even a single letter in the prescribed dhikr of prayer can invalidate it.

In this hashiya, the author clarifies several points regarding the recitations in prayer. He addresses the proper pronunciation of the ha in 'hamdah', the impermissibility of altering the wording, and the preferred phrasing for the tahmid. These refinements stem from the works of later Hanafi jurists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on changing the letter 'nun' to 'la'
In al-Walwaljiyyah, it is stated that if one replaces the nun (in 'Subhanaka') with 'la', the prayer becomes invalid, even if the tongue does not comply otherwise.
This highlights the strictness regarding the exact wording of dhikr.
2
Understand the classification of the 'ha' in 'hamdah'
According to al-Mustasfa, the 'ha' in 'hamdah' is a pronoun (damir), not a letter for pausing (saktah). This affects the pronunciation.
Hanafis distinguish between saktah and kinayah; here it is kinayah.
3
Recognize the best wording for tahmid
The preferred phrasing is 'Allahumma Rabbana wa laka al-hamd' (O Allah, our Lord, and to You is all praise). This includes the waw (and) which increases praise.
Other phrases are also acceptable but this is the best.
4
Note the difference of opinion regarding the waw
Scholars differed whether the waw in 'wa laka al-hamd' is superfluous (za'idah) or conjunctive (atf). The first view is more apparent according to al-Diraya.
This is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لقوله ) الحديث لم يذكر فيه التشهد . قوله : (لا) للكتابة وفي المستصفى أنها للضمير لا للسكت، وفي الولوالجية لو أبدل النون لا ما فسدت صلاته كما في سكب الأنهر وإن كان لسانه لا يطاوعه بتركه كما في الشرنبلالية، ولو سكن الميم من حمده فسدت صلاته كما في شرح الكيدانية عن عمدة الفتاوي قوله : (والأفضل اللهم ربنا ولك الحمد) لزيادة الثناء واختلفوا في هذه الواو فقيل زائدة وقيل : عاطفة تقديره ربنا حمدناك ولك الحمد كما في التبيين والأول أظهر كما في الدراية كذا في الشرح، وترك المرتبة الثالثة، وهي ربنا ولك الحمد. قوله : (وموجهاً أصابع رجليه نحو القبلة ولا بد من وضع إحدى القدمين، ووضع القدم، بوضع أصابعها، ويكفي وضع إصبع واحدة كذا في السيد قوله: (وجلس كل مصل بين السجدتين) ومقدار الرفع المفروض أن يكون إلى الجلوس أقرب، وهو الذي ينبغي التعويل عليه قاله السيد عازياً إلى النهر
About his saying: the hadith did not mention the tashahhud. His saying: (la) is for writing? and in al-Mustasfa it is for the pronoun, not for silence; and in al-Walwaljiyyah, if he replaces the noon (n) with la, his prayer becomes invalid as in Sirkab al-Anhur, even if his tongue does not obey him to leave it, as in al-Shurnbulaliyyah. And if he makes the meem of hamdah silent, his prayer becomes invalid, as in Sharh al-Kaydaniyyah from 'Umdat al-Fatawa. His saying: (and the best is Allahumma Rabbana wa laka al-hamd) due to increase of praise. And they differed about this waw: it was said it is superfluous, and it was said it is a conjunction, its interpretation: Our Lord, we have praised You, and to You is praise, as in al-Tabyin. The first is more apparent, as in al-Diraya, likewise in the commentary. And he omitted the third rank, which is 'Rabbana wa laka al-hamd'. His saying: (and directing his toes towards the qiblah) and it is necessary to place one of the two feet, and placing the foot is by placing its toes, and it suffices to place one toe, similarly in al-Sayyid. His saying: (and every prayer sat between the two prostrations) and the measure of the required raising is that it be closer to sitting, and that is what should be relied upon, said by al-Sayyid attributing to al-Nahr.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage contains several refinements from the hashiya. First, regarding the tasmi' and tahmid: the hadith about tashahhud is not mentioned here. The hashiya notes that changing the wording of 'Subhanaka Allahumma' or similar can invalidate prayer. For the tahmid, the best phrasing is 'Allahumma Rabbana wa laka al-hamd', which includes the waw. There is a debate whether the waw is superfluous or conjunctive; the former is stronger. The third preferred phrase is 'Rabbana wa laka al-hamd'. Second, concerning sajdah posture: it is necessary to place at least one foot with the toes directed to the qiblah; placing one toe suffices. Third, for the sitting between sajdahs, the required amount of lifting from prostration is to a position closer to sitting, not to full standing. This is the relied-upon view as quoted from al-Sayyid referring to al-Nahr.

Key Terms
الهاء للسكتة (al-ha lissaktah): The letter ha used for pausing (saktah) in recitation, not as a pronoun.
الواو الزائدة (al-waw al-za'idah): The 'waw' that is considered superfluous in the phrase 'wa laka al-hamd'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the hashiya, what is the ruling on saying 'hamd' instead of 'hamdah' in the tahmid?
A) It is permissible.
B) It invalidates the prayer.
C) It is disliked but valid.
D) It requires sajdah sahw.

Key Takeaway
Every letter and vowel in the prescribed dhikr of prayer must be observed; deliberate alteration invalidates the prayer.

Posture in Sajdah and Sitting Between Sujud
Refinements on sajdah posture and sitting between sujud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the minimum requirement for foot placement in sajdah and describe the correct posture for sitting between prostrations.

Core Idea
In sajdah, placing at least one toe on the ground suffices; the required rise from prostration is to a sitting position, not fully standing.

This lesson covers two important details from the hashiya: the minimum foot placement in sajdah and the posture for sitting between the two prostrations. These clarifications ensure that the prayer is performed correctly according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the necessity of placing the foot in sajdah
It is obligatory (la budda) to place at least one foot on the ground during sajdah, with the toes directed to the qiblah.
This is a condition for the validity of sajdah.
2
Determine the minimum requirement for toes
It suffices to place only one toe of the foot on the ground; the entire foot is not required.
This leniency is based on the ease of the religion.
3
Understand the ruling on sitting between sajdahs
The required raising from prostration should be to a posture closer to sitting, not to full standing. That is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
This applies to the person praying alone as well.
4
Note the authority for this view
Al-Sayyid attributes this ruling to al-Nahr, indicating it is the relied-upon view in the later school.
This shows the importance of later Hanafi scholars in determining the operative fatwa.

Post-Ruku Dhikr: Combining Tasmi' and Tahmid in the Hanafi School
Intra-Hanafi disputation on combining tasmi' and tahmid after ruku

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on what the imam, munfarid, and muqtadi say after ruku, and explain the preferred narration and its evidence.

Core Idea
The imam and munfarid combine tasmi' and tahmid; the muqtadi says only tahmid by consensus.

This lesson examines an intra-Hanafi dispute about the post-ruku dhikr. The text from Hashiya al-Tahtawi addresses a subtle point: should the imam and the one praying alone (munfarid) say both 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' (tasmi') and 'Rabbana laka al-hamd' (tahmid), or is one sufficient? The follower (muqtadi), however, is restricted to tahmid based on clear prophetic instruction. We will trace the different narrations, the preferred view, and the evidence that settles the practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the roles and actions
List the three roles: imam, munfarid, muqtadi. For each, determine what dhikr they say after rising from ruku according to the relied-upon Hanafi position.
The muqtadi is agreed upon; the dispute centers on imam and munfarid.
2
Unpack the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa has three narrations. State them: (1) combine tasmi' and tahmid, (2) suffice with tahmid only, (3) suffice with tasmi' only. Note which narration is chosen by later authorities like al-Hawi al-Qudsi, al-Fadli, and al-Tahawi.
The two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold the combining view.
3
Locate the dalil and its application
The primary evidence is the hadith: 'When the imam says Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah, say Rabbana laka al-hamd.' Explain how this applies to the muqtadi and why the Hanafis extend tasmi' to the imam and munfarid.
The command 'fawlū' is interpreted as sunnah, not fard, due to other evidence.
4
Determine the mufta bihi
Identify the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position: imam and munfarid combine both tasmi' and tahmid; muqtadi says only tahmid. Cite the tarjih authority (al-Hawi al-Qudsi, later scholars).
5
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Imagine you are leading a congregation or praying alone. State exactly what you should recite after ruku according to the Hanafi fatwa. Also, what if you forget? Note that missing the dhikr does not invalidate the prayer.
This dhikr is sunnah muakkadah, not wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حمد من حمده لأن السماع يذكر ويراد به القبول مجازاً كما يقال سمع الأمير كلام فلان وفي الحديث أعوذ بك من دعاء لا يسمع أي لا يستجاب والهاء للسكتة، والاستراحة لا للكناية (ربنا لك الحمد) فيجمع بين التسميع والتحميد (لو) كان إماماً) هذا قولهما، وهو رواية عن الإمام اختارها في الحاوي القدسي، وكان الفضلي والطحاوي وجماعة من المتأخرين يميلون إلى الجمع وهو قول أهل المدينة وقوله (أو منفرداً) متفق عليه على الأصح عن الإمام موافقة لهما وعنه يكتفي بالتحميد، وعنه يكتفي بالتسميع (والمقتدي يكتفي بالتحميد) اتفاقاً للأمر به في الحديث إذا قال الإمام : سمع الله لمن حمده، فقولوا ربنا لك الحمد رواه الشيخان والأفضل اللهم ربنا ولك الحمد ويليه اللهم ربنا لك الحمد ويليه ربنا لك الحمد
Praise upon His praise because hearing is mentioned and thereby acceptance is meant figuratively, as it is said, 'The amir heard the speech of so-and-so,' and in the hadith: 'I seek refuge with You from a supplication that is not heard,' i.e., not answered. The ha is for pausing and resting, not for metonymy. 'Our Lord, to You is praise' – so he combines tasmi' and tahmid. If he is an imam, this is the saying of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and it is a narration from Abu Hanifa which he chose in al-Hawi al-Qudsi. Al-Fadli, al-Tahawi, and a group of later scholars inclined toward combining, and it is the saying of the people of Madinah. His saying 'or alone' is agreed upon according to the most correct from Abu Hanifa, in agreement with the two; and from him (another narration) he suffices with tahmid, and from him he suffices with tasmi'. The muqtadi suffices with tahmid by consensus, due to the command in the hadith: 'When the imam says, “Allah hears those who praise Him,” then say, “Our Lord, to You is praise.”' Narrated by al-Bukhari and Muslim. The best is 'Allahumma Rabbana wa laka al-hamd', then 'Allahumma Rabbana laka al-hamd', then 'Rabbana laka al-hamd'.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage addresses the post-ruku dhikr: what the imam, the one praying alone (munfarid), and the follower (muqtadi) should say. The text explains a dispute within the Hanafi school. The two students (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that the imam should combine both 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' (tasmi') and 'Rabbana laka al-hamd' (tahmid). There is a narration from Abu Hanifa that agrees with them, and this is preferred by many later scholars. For the munfarid, the relied-upon position is that he also combines both. However, there are two other narrations: one where he only says tahmid, and another where he only says tasmi'. The muqtadi, by agreement, says only tahmid, based on the hadith commanding followers to say 'Rabbana laka al-hamd' when the imam says 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah'. The passage also grades the preferred wording: 'Allahumma Rabbana wa laka al-hamd' is most excellent, followed by 'Allahumma Rabbana laka al-hamd', then 'Rabbana laka al-hamd'.

Key Terms
تسميع (tasmi'): Saying 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' to announce that Allah has accepted the praise.
تحميد (tahmid): Saying 'Rabbana laka al-hamd' in response to tasmi'.
مقتدي (muqtadi): The follower in prayer, who only says tahmid after the imam's tasmi'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the muqtadi say after the imam says 'Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah' according to the relied-upon Hanafi position?
A) Sami'a Allahu liman hamidah
B) Rabbana laka al-hamd
C) Both A and B
D) Silence

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is that the imam and munfarid combine tasmi' and tahmid after ruku, while the muqtadi says only tahmid, based on the prophetic hadith and tarjih of later scholars.

Performing the First Sajdah: Takbir, Posture, and Tasbih
Ruling statement on the method of the first prostration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to describe the correct Hanafi method of performing the first prostration in prayer, including the sequence, posture, and dhikr.

Core Idea
The sunnah of sujud is a complete act: takbir, knees-first descent, tranquility, thrice tasbih, open limbs for men, closed for women.

This passage from a classical Hanafi fiqh text details the specific sunnah actions of the first prostration (sajdah) in prayer. It covers the order of movements, the required tranquility, the recommended tasbih, and the gendered differences in posture. Understanding these details helps the worshipper perform the prayer with the perfection (ihsan) taught by the Prophet ﷺ.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sequence of actions
List the steps from the passage: takbir while descending, then knees, then hands, then face between palms, then prostrate with nose and forehead.
The order is important; knees before hands is the sunnah.
2
Understand the requirement of tranquility
The phrase 'مطمئناً' (mutma'inn) means to pause briefly so all joints settle. This is a wajib element in sujud according to the Hanafi school.
Without tranquility, the sujud is considered deficient, and some scholars say invalid.
3
Memorise the tasbih and its minimum
The tasbih 'Subhana Rabbi al-A'la' should be recited at least three times. This is the minimal sunnah; more is better.
The text says 'أدناه ثلاثاً' (the minimum is three).
4
Distinguish the posture for men vs women
Men perform tajafi (keeping belly away from thighs, arms from sides). Women draw in their limbs for modesty (takhfid).
This applies in non-crowded conditions; in crowds, men also bring limbs close to avoid harming neighbors.
5
Note the finger and toe positioning
Fingers of the hands are closed together completely (yadummu kull al-damm); toes are pointed toward the qiblah.
Closing fingers is recommended only in sujud, as mercy descends and closing gathers more.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم كبر كل مصل خاراً للسجود ويختمه عند وضع جبهته للسجود ثم وضع ركبتيه ثم يديه إن لم يكن به عذر يمنعه من هذه الصفة (ثم) وضع (وجهه بين كفيه لما روينا وسجد) بأنفه وجبهته) وتقدم الحكم (مطمئناً مسبحاً) بأن يقول: سبحان ربي الأعلى مرات (ثلاثاً) وذلك أدناه لما تقدم (وجافي) أي باعد الرجل (بطنه عن فخذيه وعضديه عن إبطيه لأنه أبلغ في السجود بالأعضاء في غير زحمة وينضم فيها حذراً عن إضرار الجار (موجهاً أصابع يديه) ويضمها كل الضم لا يندب إلا هنا لأن الرحمة تنزل عليه في السجود وبالضم ينال الأكثر (و) يكون موجهاً أصابع رجليه نحو القبلة والمرأة تخفض) فتضم عضديها لجنبيها وتلزق بطنها بفخذيها لأنه أستر لها
Then every person praying says takbir while going down for prostration, and finishes it when placing his forehead to prostrate. Then he places his knees, then his hands, if he has no excuse preventing this manner. Then he places his face between his palms, and prostrates on his nose and forehead – the ruling has preceded – with tranquility, saying tasbih: 'Glory be to my Lord, the Most High' three times, which is the minimum. And he keeps his (i.e., the man's) belly away from his thighs and his arms from his sides, because that is more complete in prostration with the limbs when there is no crowding; and he brings them together (i.e., in crowding) out of caution for harming the neighbor. He directs the fingers of his hands – and he closes them together completely – this is only recommended here because mercy descends upon him in prostration and by closing he attains more. And he directs the toes of his feet toward the qiblah. The woman lowers herself, so she presses her arms to her sides and sticks her belly to her thighs because it is more concealing for her.
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
This passage details the performance of sujud. It begins with the takbir for going down (takbir al-sujud), which should reach completion as the forehead touches the ground. The order of placement is first knees, then hands, then face between the palms – unless there is a physical excuse. The prostration is on the nose and forehead (the classic Hanafi view: the forehead alone is not sufficient; both must touch). The worshipper must be tranquil (mutma'inn) and say 'Subhana Rabbi al-A'la' three times (minimum). Men are to keep their bellies away from thighs and arms away from sides to open the limbs, but in a crowd they bring them close to avoid hurting the neighbor. The fingers of the hands are to be closed together (this is the only place where full closure of fingers is recommended). The toes are directed toward the qiblah. Women do the opposite: they draw their limbs in for modesty.

Key Terms
مطمئناً (mutma'inn): Performing the prostration with calm, pausing briefly after each motion so that all joints settle.
تجافي (tajafi): Keeping the belly away from the thighs and the arms from the sides during prostration.
يضم (yadummu): To close the fingers together completely in sujud.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, what is the minimum number of times to say 'Subhana Rabbi al-A'la' in sujud?
A) One
B) Three
C) Five
D) Seven

Key Takeaway
The sunnah of sujud includes a specific order, tranquility, thrice tasbih, and gendered posture; missing tranquility invalidates the prostration.

Sitting Between the Two Prostrations and the Second Prostration
Ruling statement on the inter-sajdah sitting and second prostration

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the sitting between the two prostrations and identify the sunnah actions of the second prostration.

Core Idea
No dhikr is prescribed in the inter-sajdah sitting for fard prayers; the second sajdah repeats the first in sunnah.

In the flow of salah, after completing the first prostration, the worshipper rises to a sitting posture before the second prostration. This passage from Maraqi al-Falah clarifies the Hanafi position on this sitting—its recommended form and the absence of any prescribed remembrance. It then directs attention to the second prostration, emphasizing that the same sunnah acts are to be repeated: tranquility, tasbih, and jafa'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the posture
The worshipper sits after raising the head from the first sajdah, placing hands on thighs, with tranquility (mutma'inn). This sitting is a sunnah muakkadah.
2
Note the absence of dhikr
The text explicitly states 'ليس فيه ذكر مسنون' (there is no sunnah dhikr in it). Any reported du'a (like 'Rabbighfirli') is specifically for tahajjud, not fard prayers.
Shafi'is hold a different view; for Hanafis, remaining silent is the sunnah.
3
Observe the transition to second sajdah
After sitting, the worshipper says takbir and prostrates again. The second sajdah is a rukn (pillar) of salah.
4
List the sunnah acts of the second sajdah
The second sajdah repeats the sunnah of the first: tranquility, saying 'Subhana Rabbi al-A'la' three times (tasbih), and tajaffi (keeping belly away from thighs and arms from sides, exposing the forearms).
Jafa' is for men; women do the opposite.
5
Understand the ruling hierarchy
The sitting is sunnah muakkadah (not wajib), so if omitted, the prayer is still valid but makruh tanzihan. The second sajdah itself is fard (rukn), but the described details are sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم رفع رأسه وجلس كل مصل بين السجدتين واضعاً يديه على فخذيه مطمئناً وليس فيه ذكر مسنون والوارد فيه محمول على التهجد (ثم كبر للسجود وسجد) بعده (مطمئناً وسبح فيه) أي السجود (ثلاثاً وجافي بطنه عن فخذيه وأبدى عضديه) وهما ضبعاء والضبع بسكون الباء لا غير العضد (ثم)
Then he raises his head and sits, every person praying, between the two prostrations, placing his hands on his thighs, with tranquility. There is no prescribed dhikr (dhikr masnun) in this sitting; what is reported (of dhikr) is construed as for the night vigil (tahajjud). Then he says takbir for prostration and prostrates after it, with tranquility, and says tasbih therein three times, and keeps his belly away from his thighs and exposes his arms – the two forearms (dhaba'), the dhab' with sukun on the ba only, not the upper arm. Then...
Source pages p. 283

Commentary
After the first sajdah, the worshipper sits up between the two sajdahs with tranquility (mutma'inn). The hands are placed on the thighs. Important: there is no sunnah dhikr for this sitting; any narration mentioning du'a or dhikr refers to tahajjud prayer, not the obligatory prayer. Then he says takbir and goes into the second sajdah, repeating the same sunnah actions as the first: tranquility, saying 'Subhana Rabbi al-A'la' three times, and keeping the belly away from thighs and arms from sides (jafa'). The text clarifies that the exposed part is the forearms (dhaba'), not the upper arm (adud). The passage ends with 'Then' leading to rising for the next rak'ah.

Key Terms
الجلسة بين السجدتين (al-jalsah bayna al-sajdatayn): The sitting between the first and second prostration; a sunnah with no prescribed dhikr in fard prayers.
تجافي (tajafi): Keeping the belly away from thighs and arms from sides; already defined, but here applied to both sajdahs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Subhana Rabbi al-A'la
B) Rabbighfirli
C) None
D) Allahu Akbar

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school teaches that the inter-sajdah sitting has no prescribed dhikr for fard prayers, and the second prostration repeats the same sunnah acts as the first: tranquility, tasbih, and jafa'.

Rising for the Second Rak'ah: Omitted Actions and Sunnah of Raising Hands
Ruling statement with expansion on raising hands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three actions omitted in the second rak'ah and list the occasions where raising hands is sunnah according to the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
In the second rak'ah, omit thana, ta'awwudh, and raising hands; raise hands only in specific places.

The second rak'ah of prayer is identical to the first except for three omissions: the opening praise (thana), the seeking of refuge (ta'awwudh), and the raising of hands. The Hanafi school emphasizes that raising hands is not a general sunnah in prayer but is limited to specific occasions, such as the opening takbir, qunut, Eid takbirs, and during supplications at certain places. This lesson will clarify these rulings and their basis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling for rising to the second rak'ah
The worshipper rises directly with takbir, without leaning on hands or sitting (jalsat al-istiraha) unless excused.
Jalsat al-istiraha is sunnah according to Shafi'is, not Hanafis.
2
List the actions of the second rak'ah that are identical to the first
Recitation of Fatihah, surah, ruku, sujud, etc. are all performed as in the first rak'ah.
3
Specify the three things omitted in the second rak'ah
Thana (opening praise), ta'awwudh (seeking refuge), and raising hands (raf' al-yadayn).
These omissions occur because the assembly (majlis) has not changed and the opening act is only for the start.
4
Enumerate the established occasions for raising hands in Hanafi fiqh
At the opening takbir of every prayer, at the qunut takbir in witr, at the extra takbirs in Eid prayers, when seeing the Ka'bah, when touching the Black Stone, when standing on Safa and Marwah, at 'Arafah, Muzdalifah, after stoning the Jamarat, during istisqa', and after the post-prayer tasbih.
5
Note the cross-madhhab difference
Shafi'is raise hands at every takbir in prayer; Hanafis restrict it to the listed occasions.
Each school has its proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

He raises his head saying takbir for rising, i.e., standing for the second rak'ah, without supporting himself on the ground with his hands if he has no excuse, and without sitting before standing that is called 'jalsat al-istiraha' and is considered sunnah by al-Shafi'i. The second rak'ah he performs like the first – you know what it includes – except that he does not recite thana (because it is only for opening) nor ta'awwudh (since the assembly has not changed) nor raise his hands, because raising hands is not sunnah in the states of ruku' and standing (except at the opening of each prayer, at the takbir for qunut in witr, and at the extra takbirs for the two 'Eids, due to unanimous reports. The manner of raising is to the level of the ears. And raising them outstretched toward the sky while supplicating is sunnah when he stands on al-Safa and al-Marwah, likewise at the standing at 'Arafah and at the standing at Muzdalifah, and at the standing after stoning the first and the middle Jamrah, as established in the noble Sunnah. And they are raised in the supplication for rain (istisqa') and similar, because raising hands in supplication is sunnah. Likewise at his supplication after finishing the tasbih, tahmid, and takbir that we will mention following the prayers, as is the practice of Muslims in all lands.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
When rising for the second rak'ah, the worshipper says takbir and rises directly without leaning on the ground (if possible) and without a sitting (jalsat al-istiraha), which is sunnah according to the Shafi'i school but not in the Hanafi school for the able person. The second rak'ah is identical in its actions to the first, except for three omissions: no thana (opening praise), no ta'awwudh (seeking refuge), and no raising of hands. The text then digresses to elaborate on the specific instances where raising hands is sunnah, which include the opening takbir, qunut takbir in witr, Eid takbirs, seeing the Ka'bah, touching the Black Stone, making du'a on Safa/Marwah and at certain hajj sites, istisqa', and du'a after the post-prayer tasbih. This list is a summary of Hanafi rulings on the matter.

Key Terms
جلسة الاستراحة (jalsat al-istiraha): A brief sitting before standing in prayer, adopted by Shafi'is; Hanafis do not do it without excuse.
ثناء (thana'): The opening praise 'Subhanaka Allahumma' etc., recited only in the first rak'ah.
تعوذ (ta'awwudh): Saying 'A'udhu billahi min al-shaytan al-rajim', omitted in second rak'ah because the assembly does not change.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Raising hands
D) Fatihah

Key Takeaway
The second rak'ah repeats the first except for the opening praise, seeking refuge, and raising hands; raising hands is limited to specific occasions.

Occasions for Raising Hands in the Hanafi School
List of sunnah positions for raising hands

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the specific occasions inside and outside prayer where raising hands is sunnah according to the Hanafi school and describe the manner of raising.

Core Idea
Raising hands is sunnah only at opening takbir, qunut, Eid takbirs, and many du'a occasions outside prayer.

This passage from Hashiya al-Tahtawi clarifies the Hanafi position on raising hands during worship. It lists the few instances in prayer and many outside prayer where this action is a confirmed sunnah, distinguishing the school from others who raise hands more frequently.

Guided Path
1
Understand the General Ruling
The passage begins with a negation: 'Raising hands is not sunnah except...' This establishes that the default is not to raise hands; only specific places are exceptional. Identify this principle.
2
Identify Prayer-Related Occasions
List the three instances within formal prayer: opening takbir of every prayer, takbir of qunut in witr, and the extra takbirs of the two 'Eids.
These are the only times hands are raised during prayer itself.
3
Identify Non-Prayer Occasions
Outside prayer, hands are raised in specific moments of supplication: seeing the Ka'bah, touching the Black Stone, standing on Safa and Marwah, standing at 'Arafah and Muzdalifah, after stoning the jammarat, during istisqa', and after the regular tasbih following prayers.
These occasions are all associated with du'a.
4
Note the Manner of Raising
The text specifies that hands are raised to the level of the ears (hidha' al-udhunain). For du'a, they are outstretched toward the sky; for takbirs, palms face forward toward the qiblah.
This manner applies uniformly to all listed occasions.
5
Recognize Cross-Madhhab Difference
The Hanafi school restricts hand-raising to these few places, while the Shafi'i school raises hands at every takbir of the prayer. This difference stems from varying interpretations of hadith.
Knowing this prevents confusion when praying behind an imam of another madhhab.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا يسن رفع اليدين إلا عند افتتاح كل صلاة وعند تكبير القنوت في الوتر وتكبيرات الزوائد في العيدين لاتفاق الأخبار وصفة الرفع فيها حذو الأذنين ويسن رفعهما مبسوطتين نحو السماء حين يرى الكعبة المشرفة أي وقت معاينتها فتكون العين في فقعس للعيدين ومعاينة البيت للدعاء وهو مستجاب ويسن رفعهما حين يستلم الحجر الأسود مستقبلاً بباطنهما الحجر ويسن رفعهما مبسوطتين نحو السماء داعياً حين يقوم على الصفا والمروة و كذلك عند الوقوف بعرفة و وقوف مزدلفة و في الوقوف بعد رمي الجمرة الأولى و الجمرة الوسطى كما ورد بذلك السنة الشريفة وترفع في دعاء الاستسقاء ونحوه لأن رفع اليد في الدعاء سنة و كذلك عند دعائه بعد فراغه من التسبيح والتحميد والتكبير الذي سنذكره عقب الصلوات كما عليه المسلمون في سائر البلدان
Raising hands is not sunnah except at the opening of every prayer, at the takbir of qunut in witr, and at the extra takbirs in the two 'Eids, due to unanimous reports. The manner of raising is to the level of the ears. It is sunnah to raise them outstretched toward the sky when one sees the noble Ka'bah, i.e., at the time of beholding it. ... And it is sunnah to raise them when one touches the Black Stone, facing its inner side to the stone. And it is sunnah to raise them outstretched toward the sky while supplicating when one stands on al-Safa and al-Marwah, likewise at the standing at 'Arafah and the standing at Muzdalifah, and in the standing after stoning the first and the middle Jamrah, as established in the noble Sunnah. And they are raised in the supplication for rain (istisqa') and similar, because raising hands in supplication is sunnah. Likewise at his supplication after finishing the tasbih, tahmid, and takbir that we will mention following the prayers, as is the practice of Muslims in all lands.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage systematically lists the occasions when raising the hands is considered sunnah in the Hanafi school. It begins by negating the general practice of raising hands at every takbir (as done in other schools). Instead, it is sunnah only at the opening takbir of each prayer, at the qunut takbir in witr, and at the extra takbirs in Eid prayers. The hands are raised to the level of the ears. Outside of prayer, raising hands in supplication is sunnah when seeing the Ka'bah, touching the Black Stone, making du'a on Safa and Marwah, at Arafat, Muzdalifah, after throwing jamarat, in istisqa', and after the regular tasbih following prayers. This list integrates acts of worship inside and outside formal prayer, showing the comprehensive sunnah related to raising hands.

Key Terms
رفع اليدين (raf' al-yadayn): Lifting the hands during takbir or du'a; sunnah in specific contexts.
حذو الأذنين (hidha' al-udhunain): Raising hands to the level of the earlobes, with palms facing forward.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Raising hands is sunnah only at specific times in prayer (opening, qunut, Eid) and many times outside prayer (seeing Ka'bah, touching Black Stone, du'a on Safa/Marwah, at Arafat, Muzdalifah, after jamarat, istisqa', after prayer tasbih).

First Tashahhud: Posture, Recitation, and Indication
Ruling statement on tashahhud posture and recitation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on the posture, recitation, and finger indication for the first tashahhud, and distinguish between what is wajib and what is sunnah.

Core Idea
Tashahhud is wajib; its posture and finger motion are sunnah.

This lesson dissects the Hanafi rulings for the first tashahhud, covering the specific postures for men and women, the obligatory recitation, and the recommended finger indication. The passage from Maraqi al-Falah clarifies what is sunnah versus wajib, ensuring your salah aligns with the school's precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal category of tashahhud
Determine that reciting tashahhud is wajib (not fard) in the Hanafi school. This means if omitted, sajdah al-sahw is required.
2
Differentiate between men's and women's postures
Men sit iftirash (on left foot, right foot erect). Women sit tawarruk (on left hip). This distinction is based on the sunnah.
3
Analyze the recitation requirement
The tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud must be recited with the intention of greeting. No additional dhikr is added in the first sitting.
4
Examine the finger indication
The sunnah is to point with the right index finger, raising it at 'La ilaha' and lowering at 'illa Allah'. This is the 'sahih' view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا فرغ الرجل من سجدتي الركعة الثانية افترش رجله اليسرى، وجلس عليها ونصب يمناه ووجه أصابعها نحو القبلة، ووضع يديه على فخذيه (وبسط أصابعه) وجعلها منتهية إلى رأس ركبتيه (والمرأة تتورك) وقدمنا صفته (وقرأ) المصلي ولو مقتدياً (تشهد ابن مسعود رضي الله عنه) ويقصد معانيه مرادة له على أنه ينشئها تحية وسلاماً منه (وأشار بالمسيحة من أصابعه اليمنى في الشهادة على الصحيح يرفعها عند النفي ويضعها عند الإثبات ولا يزيد على التشهد في القعود الأول) لوجوب القيام للثالثة، وهو كما قال : علمني رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم التشهد أخذ كفي بين كفيه كما يعلمني السورة من القرآن فقال إذا قعد أحدكم في الصلاة فليقل التحيات الله والصلوات والطيبات جمع تحية من حيا فلان فلاناً إذا دعا له عند ملاقاته لقولهم حياك الله أي أبقاك الله والمراد هنا أعز الألفاظ التي تدل على الملك والعظمة، وكل عبادة قولية الله تعالى والمراد بالصلوات هنا العبادات البدنية، ونحوها، والطيبات العبادات المالية الله تعالى وهي الصادرة منه ليلة الإسراء فلما قال ذلك النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم بإلهام من الله تعالى رد الله عليه وحياه بقوله السلام عليك أيها النبي ورحمة الله وبركاته) فقابل التحيات بالسلام الذي هو تحية الإسلام وقابل الصلوات بالرحمة التي هي بمعناها
And when the man finishes the two prostrations of the second rak'ah, he spreads his left foot and sits on it, erects his right foot, directs its toes toward the qiblah, places his hands on his thighs, spreads his fingers, and makes them reach the top of his knees. The woman sits mutawarrikan (on her left hip), and we have already described its description. The worshipper, even if a muqtadi, recites the tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with him), intending its meanings for himself, as if uttering a greeting and salutation. And he points with the palm (or finger) of his right hand during the shahada (testimony) – according to the correct view, he raises it at the negation and lowers it at the affirmation. He does not add anything to the tashahhud in the first sitting, due to the obligation of standing for the third rak'ah. It is as he said: The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) taught me the tashahhud, taking my palm between his hands as he taught me a surah from the Qur'an, saying: 'When one of you sits in prayer, let him say: al-tahiyatu lillahi wa al-salawatu wa al-tayyibatu...' Then he explained the words: 'tahiyyah' is from the greeting, 'salawat' are bodily acts of worship, 'tayyibat' are financial acts of worship. Allah replied to him with 'al-salamu alayka ayyuha al-nabiyyu wa rahmatullahi wa barakatuhu'.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage details the posture for tashahhud after the second rak'ah before standing. Men sit on the left foot (iftrash) with the right foot erect (nasb), toes pointing toward the qiblah. Hands are on thighs with fingers spread. Women sit on the left hip (tawarruk) as previously described. The tashahhud of Ibn Mas'ud is recited – the most authentic version taught by the Prophet. The worshipper should intend the meanings of the words as a greeting from him. He points with his finger during the shahada: raising it at 'La ilaha' and lowering at 'illa Allah' (according to the correct view). No additional dhikr is added in the first tashahhud because one must stand for the third rak'ah immediately after. The commentary then elaborates on the wording of the tashahhud, linking the Prophet's Isra' journey and the divine response. This reinforces the sunnah nature of this specific tashahhud.

Key Terms
افتراش (iftirash): Sitting on the left foot with the right foot erect; the preferred posture for men in tashahhud.
تورك (tawarruk): Sitting on the left hip, feet together and protruding to the right; prescribed for women in all sittings.
إشارة بالمسيحة (isharah bil-musabbihah): Pointing with the right index finger during the shahada; raising at 'La ilaha' and lowering at 'illa Allah'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How should a man sit for the first tashahhud according to the Hanafi school?
A) Sitting on both heels (iq'a')
B) Tawarruk on left hip
C) Iftirash: sitting on left foot with right foot erect
D) Cross-legged

Key Takeaway
The first tashahhud is wajib; men sit iftirash, women tawarruk; the finger points at negation and affirmation.

Salah — Prayer
intermediate
6 min
Rising for the Second Rak'ah and Omissions
Rulings on rising for the second rak'ah and omissions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on rising for the second rak'ah and identify which acts are not repeated in the second rak'ah, explaining the rationale.

Core Idea
In the second rak'ah, thana', ta'awwudh, and hand-raising are omitted; jalsat al-istiraha is not sunnah.

This passage from al-Tahtawi's hashya clarifies two aspects of the transition to the second rak'ah: the physical movement (rising without leaning on hands, no jalsat al-istiraha) and the omissions in recitation and gestures (no thana', ta'awwudh, or raising hands). The Hanafi school views jalsat al-istiraha as not sunnah—though permissible with excuse—contrary to the Shafi'i school which considers it sunnah mu'akkadah. This lesson will help you internalize the Hanafi position and distinguish it from the Shafi'i school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of rising for the second rak'ah
The worshipper rises from sajdah with takbir. The recommended method is to stand directly without leaning on hands unless there is an excuse, and without a brief sitting (jalsat al-istiraha).
Jalsat al-istiraha is a sitting after rising from the first prostration before standing for the next rak'ah.
2
Note the status of jalsat al-istiraha
According to the Hanafi school, jalsat al-istiraha is not sunnah. It is permissible for those with an excuse (e.g., elderly or ill) but otherwise not recommended. The Shafi'i school holds it as sunnah mu'akkadah.
This is a key difference between the two schools.
3
Recognize that the second rak'ah replicates the first, with stated omissions
Everything performed in the first rak'ah (fatihah, surah, ruku', sujud) is repeated in the second rak'ah, except for three specific acts: thana', ta'awwudh, and raising hands.
The text says 'except that he does not recite thana' because it is only for the opening...'
4
List the three omissions and their reasons
Thana' is omitted because it is prescribed only at the very beginning of prayer. Ta'awwudh is omitted because the 'sitting place' (i.e., the transition from qiyam to qiyam) does not change. Raising hands is omitted because it is not sunnah in these movements (only sunnah in takbirat al-ihram, qunut, and 'eid takbirs).
Raising hands in bowing and standing is a Shafi'i sunnah, not Hanafi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رفع كتاب الصلاة رأسه مكبراً للنهوض أي القيام للركعة الثانية بلا اعتماد على الأرض بيديه إن لم يكن به عذر وبلا قعود قبل القيام يسمى جلسة الاستراحة عند الشافعي سنة والركعة الثانية يفعل فيها كالأولى وعلمت ما شملته إلا أنه أي المصلي لا يثني لأنه للاستفتاح فقط، ولا يتعوذ لعدم تبدل المجلس ولا يرفع يديه إذ لا يسن رفع اليدين
Raising his head with takbir to stand up, meaning to stand for the second rak'ah without relying on the ground with his hands unless he has an excuse, and without sitting before standing called jalsat al-istiraha which according to al-Shafi'i is sunnah. And in the second rak'ah he does as in the first, and you know what it includes, except that he does not recite thana' because it is only for the opening, and he does not recite ta'awwudh because the sitting place does not change, and he does not raise his hands because raising hands is not sunnah.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from al-Tahtawi's hashya on Maraqi al-Falah, expanding on the matn. It covers two rulings: (1) how the worshipper rises from sujud for the second rak'ah: he should not lean on his hands unless he has an excuse, and he should not perform jalsat al-istiraha (a brief sitting), which is sunnah according to al-Shafi'i but not according to the Hanafi school. (2) In the second rak'ah, the worshipper does everything as in the first, except that he does not recite thana' (the opening supplication) because it is prescribed only at the very beginning of prayer, and he does not recite ta'awwudh because the 'sitting place' (i.e., the transition from qiyam to qiyam) does not change. He also does not raise his hands because raising hands is not sunnah in these movements (it is only sunnah in specific occasions). The Hanafi position is that these actions are not sunnah in these positions; jalsat al-istiraha is not sunnah (it is a dispensation for those with excuse).

Key Terms
جلسة الاستراحة (jalsat al-istiraha): A brief sitting after rising from the first prostration before standing for the next rak'ah, considered sunnah by al-Shafi'i but not by Hanafis.
الثناء (al-thana'): The opening supplication after takbirat al-ihram (e.g., Subhanaka Allahumma...), recited only at the beginning of the prayer, not repeated in later rak'ahs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second rak'ah of a fard prayer, which of the following does the Hanafi school consider not sunnah to repeat?
A) Recitation of al-Fatihah
B) Recitation of thana' and ta'awwudh
C) Bowing and prostration
D) Recitation of tashahhud

Key Takeaway
In the second rak'ah, the Hanafi school omits thana', ta'awwudh, and hand-raising; jalsat al-istiraha is not sunnah but permissible with excuse.
